630439845X|2.0|Let me be clear, I got ahuge lump in my throat when I saw this movie.  It's beautifully shot, realistically grubby and the violence is not at hair out of place for the period or for modern New Zealand (on a spiritual if not &quot;Once Were Warriors&quot; level).  Holly Hunter, Harvey Keitel, Sam Neill and Anna Paquin were all stupendous.<br />Campion here presents us with a wonderfully expatriate view of New Zealand as it was, and to her credit, uncovers many of the dirty secrets in the NZ psyche.<br />In fact, as a movie about white New Zealanders, this was a 5 star piece of cinema.  With the right foregrounding you can see many of the things that are intensely problematic about New Zealand society both historically and now: the blinkered provincialism, the contempt for indigenous people (except as trendy accessories), the horrific valorization of commerce over art (anti-intellectual, anti-artistic and anti-spiritual) and the systematic devaluing of women and love of violence that thrive in the standard blokes-are-us set of understandings of NZ both past and present that you can find in any pub or wine bar in any part of the country on a Friday night.<br />That being said, the movie also plays the natives for laughs and inexplicably places the character of Bain as a partially-tattooed culture broker for Maori who have English muskets but apparently no command of the English language themselves.  While figures like Bain were certainly important during the early years of the New Zealand colony (1800-1840), the following decades when the film is set saw their importance decline rapidly. In any case, relations between Maori and European for almost the whole of the 19th century were much more intensive and intimate than this movie makes out.<br />This historical elision is actually important because it provides a way for white people to be the sole group at the center of the movie and ignores the fundamental inter-relatedness of the Maori and European communities during this time period.  In this vein, Campion can make a movie which does not challenge our understanding of European settlement of this (and other countries) as a heroic endeavor against the odds in some sort of cultural vacuum.<br />For the record, during the intial phases of English settlement in Aotearoa, Maori in many areas became fluent in English (at least to trade) very quickly.  If Sam Neill's little outpost had been established long enough to have an amateur dramatic society there would have been more than a dozen local men and women who would have been fluent enough and canny enough to be giving the English a hefty run for their money.<br />Maori successes in commerce (the Waikato tribes for instance were sending tons of salt meat, potatoes and other vegetables to Australia on their own merchant ships by the early 1860s) actually led to colonists deliberately starting wars with Maori in order to eliminate superior Maori economic competition. This probably qualifies as one of the most blatant interferences in the free market (too bad there wasn't a World Trade Organization back then!). Maori in some parts of the country owned flour mills, fishing vessels, and toll booths and ordered china tea sets from England.<br />We have no notion of English barbarism in their own country during this period either.  The subtext is that we do not need to know about the burgeoning Industrial Revolution with its smoke stacks and polluted rivers, the Poor Laws which forced debtors into prison and made hundreds of small farmers destitute, the riots over bread, the public hangings or the sale of small children in the streets of London.  Like Martians, Holly Hunter and her piano are transported to the beach, no one cares where from.  It is the quintessential Euro-settler fantasy: &quot;I come from nowhere with nothing, land here and make the earth productive with my own bare hands and bring civilization.&quot;<br />What this movie shamelessly replicates is a Kiplingesque story ofplucky European triumph over the native landscape (and the Philistine colonists as well to be sure - here Sam Neill is placed outside civilization as surely as the Maori).  Holly Hunter's character finds true love in a restored genteel setting without any thought to the costs paid by the native people.<br />It's a great mood peice but lousy history. Thank you sir, may I have another?ry of plucky European triumph over the native landscape (and the Philistine colonists as well to be sure - here Sam Neill is placed outside civilization as surely as the Maori).  Holly Hunter's character finds true love in a restored genteel setting without any thought to the costs paid by the native people.<br />It's a great mood peice but lousy history. Thank you sir, may I have another?

B00005B36H|5.0|I make no bones about it.  I was about ready to lose it throughout the last year as the release date for Tool's new CD. With my luck, I'd end up losing my hearing in a freak accident by the time the CD hit the market.  Well, my hearing is still in tact and IT WAS WORTH THE WAIT!  LATERALUS is amazing.<br />This CD contains some of their most intricate work to date.  It is one that will be worthy of anyone's &quot;Desert Island&quot; list.  It will also be keeping musical scholars in the woodshed as they try to determine what and how many time signatures the band can actually merge into a song while keeping a solid structure. As a band, Tool has an uncanny ability to express both an amazing, dead on sense of tightness as a band in addition to impressive looseness that keeps you from thinking that their songs are merely exercises in musical gymnastics.  You really get an idea of how well these musicians lock into one another's grooves.  It's like a harmonic family...sometimes dissonant, sometimes like an orchestra.  8 seconds into the first tack (&quot;The Grudge&quot;), you get hit with the sonic blast of Danny's drums with Justin and Adam syncopating riffs on bass and guitar.  Maynard's immediately recognizable voice kicks in soon after that and you can't help but realize that this is what many a fan has waited for over the past five years.  Adam's guitar work throughout shows a higher level of complexity and a much more melodic quality.  Justin's bass work locks dead on at times with Danny and back to Adam (&quot;Schism&quot; for example, where he harmonizes with Adam's guitar on the intro and leads into the riff that forms the song).  Maynard brings his amazing voice to the table and shows the amazing range in abilities, ranging from the melodic qualities in &quot;The Patient&quot; and the title track to fits of rage that could skin the membrane of your heart in &quot;Ticks and Leeches.&quot;  By far, his voice is one of the most versatile in the music world.  Danny Carrey's name will rank up with the likes of Bruford, Cobham, Peart &amp; (fill in the blank for any drummers with bottomless pockets), and this should catapult him beyond hero status.  Virtually every song (aside from a couple of the tracks that are not musical, like &quot;Mantra&quot;) is driven by his inhuman abilities.  He IS the new breed of drummer, and you won't find him doing anything that he cannot do live..if you hear it on the album, he can do it equally as impressive on stage, something that many bands desperately need to learn.  Aside from drummers like Carter Beauford (DMB), he is virtually peerless in a category of elite drummers to gain prominence in the last ten years.<br />I obviously like this CD and have listened to it nonstop while I wrote this review (having picked it up a day ahead of release (it helps to know your local independent record store owner!).  I am biased, since I started listening to a band as a DJ in '92 when I got a promo copy of Opiate w/ a press release while working in a college radio station and have been a fan ever since (and the CD got airplay, but never was returned to the station's library..hmmm).   As a guitarist for over twenty years, I found it odd that I was driven to band that was not a &quot;guitar hero&quot; band...maybe that is why I became such a fan.  This band is a unit, not just a vehicle for any one member.  This is also a band that has not been so prolific as to release material that insults their fans with commercialized junk for the sake of record sales at the demands of a label or agent.  Finally, this is a band that, even after nearly three years off of touring, sold out their first leg of shows in the US in less time than it would take you to listen to the first track off the album. This album is an amazing follow-up to Ænima (and for those that were not happy about the interludes on Ænima, there is only one &quot;non-musical&quot; track on Lateralus).  It would be hard to pick a favorite track.   I'd have to say the title track has grown on me due to airplay over the last week, then &quot;Ticks and Leeches&quot; and &quot;Schism&quot; were the first to grab me. Of course, as with all of their albums, it becomes infectious as a whole and I would have to say that to definitively single out any song without more listening/playing wouldn't do it justice.  Do yourself a favor and buy the CD and hear it for yourself (and to any drummers out there, do your car a favor and cover your dashboard before you listen to this in traffic, because you'll beat it silly).  I'll be first in line to get tickets when they come back from overseas in July, and I'm sure that most who buy this CD will be doing the same.

B00005B36H|5.0|Thankfully, Tool aren't one of the bands that you expect to make the same album again and again.  Each new release, sparse as they are, moves the sound and approach of the band on in a new direction.  And for all of you people worried that Lateralus might just be a re-tread of old territory... It's not.  It's very hard to give an in-depth review of something that's only been in your posession for 24 hours, but I feel that I've got enough of a grip now to write a bit.  The things that immediately hit me:  1) Production.  It's less &quot;mushy&quot; than Aenima.  Not suggesting that Aenima was badly produced, the sound worked great for the music.  But Lateralus has cleaner sounds, generally.  Adam Jones' guitar is still huge and crunchy, but it doesn't obscure the rhythm section in the way that it used to.  The drum sound is superb, and the variety of lovely bass tones on the album are a nice surprise.  I have to admit, &quot;lovely&quot; was never a word I'd previously associated with Tool and bass.  2) Maynard sounds quite a bit different.  In the same way that his vocals changed between Undertow and Aenima, they've shifted again here.  There's a fair bit of stuff that sounds like A Perfect Circle, but there's also some viscious distorted screaming that sounds harsher than anything I can recall on Aenima.  3) Rhythm.  This album goes through almost every time signature in the book!  The album kicks off in 5/8 (or 3/4 subdivided into quintuplets.. you choose!) on The Grudge, and stays similarly obtuse for much of the album.  You've got to love a band this popular that releases a single with sections in 13/8!  4) Long songs.  Sometimes they hold sections for what seems like a very long time, longer than on any of the previous releases.  These tracks worked better for me when I was lying in bed in the dark, with the music up floor-shakingly loud.  It's almost like listening to a drum&amp;bass track - you don't expect fast changes, more a hypnotic wash.  5) Great quietsections!  Eon Blue Apocalypse vaguely reminded me of some of Bill Frisell's guitar tones on Grand Guignol (the track, not the album) by John Zorn/Naked City, and the other &quot;segue&quot; tracks (like Mantra) are equally pretty.   All in all, a lot to get your ears around.  Considering the level of anticipation surrounding this album, the fact that it's not a massive let-down is a vast achievement in itself.  And I'm still laughing over the fact that Schism was the #1 new release in terms of radio airplay in the states.  Seven minutes long, with some slightly baffling odd time signatures and lyrics that aren't exactly of the &quot;I did it all for the nookie&quot; variety.  I love these guys!

B00005B36H|5.0|With Lateralus, Tool has advanced their music and restored the faith of the vast horde of rock fans who have wandered aimlessly for the past 5 years. For everyone who is left out by nu-metal and yo-core like Limp Bizkit, Creed, Papa Rocah and Kid Rock, Tool has produced a dense, intricate album that jumps off from their earlier work and pushes into new territory. You'll hear hints of Aenima in Schism and The Grudge, hints of Opiate in Ticks &amp; Leeches and hints of Salival in The Patient. But while the players are the same, the attitude is different, the lyrics deeper and the relationships between the instruments clearer. By the time the three song tapestry that closes the CD in Pink Floyd territory finally comes to a halt, you have the idea that these echoes of the past are the final remnant of 20th century rock being put to bed so Tool can build a whole new style out of the wreckage.

The film is also very real which definitley helps add to the pervading sense of loss. There are no Holywood touches to Hanks' survival, he lights his own fires (after three days of trying), bleeds when he steps on a reef and doesn't have batteries that last in his flashlight for four years. Unlike other stranded in nature films like &quot;The Edge,&quot; where Anthony Hopkins and Alec Baldwin are seen sporting bear coats right after killing a bear with a stick, Castaway is about as real to life as you can imagine. <br />There are of course some flaws. Helen Hunt is pretty poor as well, her only purpose seems to be to taunt Hanks by chasing him down in the rain to only kiss him once and then go back to her new husband. These are minor quibbles to another wise great movie. Castway is real and the sense of desperation and loneliness it creates is uncommon in movies of this scope. <br />Take it for what it is and don't be expecting Hanks to be setting tiger traps for pirates.

B00005B36H|4.0|Tool is serious. There is no doubt about that. They are not about stage gimmicks, T-shirts and MTV specials. They *are* some of the most wildly inventive musicians to ever toil in the genre of rock music. Just listen to the opening notes of &quot;The Grudge&quot;. The twisted guitar tone wails behind the mindboggling bass... and it just gets better. Maynard's almost-angleic singing is mixed way down, so he sounds like he's singing from a cave or the bottom of a well. An eerie and interesting sonic effect. The album sounds like it is meant to be heard as one coherent whole... a symphony of sorts, dealing with alternating waves of roaring rage, simmering regret, split-second epiphanies of unspeakable beauty. The most startling and original structural point  comes with the twin songs &quot;Parbol&quot; &quot;Parbola&quot; which approach the same musical ideas with vastly different approach. I would say one is the &quot;evil twin&quot;, but I'm not sure which is the angel and which is the devil yet.  The creepy, dissonant opening of &quot;The Patient&quot; is classic Tool, but the mindblowing &quot;Ticks and Leeches&quot; seems like the highpoint of an already exalted journey. When I got to that song on my first listen, I was thinking, &quot;even if the rest of the album is awful, this will still be one of the best rock albums in years&quot; and then, Tool unleashes what is probably thier greatest song. <br />Really, one could not imagine musicians playing on a more elevated and stunningly precise level. They sound like they are in absolute control of every strangled note. They play together with a kind of near-empathy that is indeed rarefied. A powerful, harrowing record; It demands a lot of the listener. I'll probably find myself listening to this almost exclusively for the next few weeks on headphones and speakers, trying to unravel each sonic stitch of this sublime and horrific, and ultimately beautiful tapestry.

So I took a deep breath, placed the cd in the tray (all 78 plus minutes of it),slipped on the headphones and pressed play.<br />So what's it like? I guess that is only a question that can honestly be answered by the individual listener, but to these ears it transcended mere music and moved things into another realm altogether. All the albums that I has purchased recently suddenly became exposed as the worthless imposters thay almost certainly are, and I realised that I was in the presence of something very special. Hey, I'm a biased Tool fan, but this is truly one of those once in a lifetime albums. As usual, the artwork is first class, and the stlye and look of the CD cover is awesome. B00005BL2A|4.0|What is typical of DM music? Old fans will probably say: heavy synth arrangement, catchy rhythm, lyrics that doesn't really make sense but rhymes perfectly. However, this conventional understanding of DM music does not apply to this album...not at all. In 'Exciter', DM abandons its trademark style that has made it so popular during the 80s and 90s to experiment with a more ambient and moodier synth arrangement that has a touch of industrial, rock and even jazz. On the first listening, old fans will probably feel betrayed and disappointed. Gone are the dancy synth rhythms. The whole album is dominated by slower tracks with the exception of the industrial-sounding 'The Dead of Night' (an 'I Feel You' sounding track which almost has a touch of N.I.N) and the only upbeat tune ('I Feel Loved') which actually sounds nothing like their traditional fast tunes. Nonetheless, this album builds slowly upon the listener. The moody arrangement on 'Dream On', 'When the Body Speaks', 'Freelove' and 'Goodnight Lovers' actually works perfectly with Dave's voice. 'Breathe' is also an interesting track featuring Martin on vocals with a jazzy background. On the whole, this album presents a fresh new sound for DM, except it's a 'moodier' Mode rather than an 'exciter'.

Fascinating concept, but with its glut of overblown special effects ultimately lapsing into SciFi cheese, and plot turns folding in on themselves until they collapse under their own weight, this movie was just another glaring example of Hollywood 2000: self indulgent FX, convolluted plots, mostly crummy music, and a movie at least a half hour too long for its storyline, finally culminating in a lousy ending. When Hollywood finally learns that &quot;less is more&quot; it may very well be one of the final signs of the apocalypse. Sometimes I long for the days when they could make a good 90-minute movie (and I'm really not that old). <br />This movie actually got my interest back, and lost it again, at least twice over the course of the circuitous storyline, but too often fell back on old SciFi cliches, lengthy explanatory soliloquys to TELL what the convoluted plot couldn't possibly SHOW, and flashy special FX, all of which made me long for one of those good old half hour Twilight Zone, &quot;is reality just a dream&quot; episodes.  I am not nearly as much of a movie buff as my wife (she thought it sucked, too, though), and my idea of good SciFi is old TZ, 2001: A Space Odyssey, and Planet of the Apes, but I think this is may be the worst SciFi movie I've seen in at least 20 years.<br />At one point, at just before the two hour mark in the movie, I said to my wife, who was almost dozing by that time, &quot;Okay, this has almost become a decent movie, but if it goes on for another half hour, it sucks.&quot; She said, &quot;You know it will, they're notgoing to end it here.&quot; Sure enough, a half hour later I'm looking at another stupid, non sequitur Hollywood ending--all-too-appropriately accompanied by some &quot;Nu Metal&quot; crap for end title music. What I find much more societally unsettling than this movie's scenario, is that so many people thought this movie was great. I wonder how many of those people were able to follow the storyline at all; and how many just liked it because of the glut of FX, the idea of artificial intelligence taking over reality, and yet another in a long line of Nu Metal and Industrial-dominated soundtracks. The REAL Matrix is an entertainment industry that keeps feeding stuff like this to an audience that just keeps mindlessly saying, &quot;Thank you, sir, might I have another?&quot;

0385484518|5.0|Many of us have had great teachers; people who taught us various subjects and helped us to understand many concepts. Fewer of us have had the opportunity to have a professor that teaches us to love mathematics, strive to be the best writers we can, or work to change the future by wholly understanding the past. Once in a lifetime is there a teacher who can make more than one student understand the greatest lessons life has to offer. In Mitch Albom's Tuesdays with Morrie, the reader becomes the student and is able to learn those difficult, often unanswered, questions surrounding the meaning of love and life and death. The author was able to turn the last weeks he had with a beloved professor and true friend into the most wonderful course that can never be taught or planned in a curriculum. This lesson cannot be taught in the most elite high school settings or even in the lecture halls of an Ivy League School. This thesis is a gift to all who read it because of its ability to make the reader feel as if she is sitty next to the bed of the dying Morrie Schwartz and examining her own life as he tells about what he has learned from his. In a society where many people feel the need to be strong and constantly moving, it is also difficult to speak freely, honestly, about any subject close to the heart or tied to emotions. Not only did Morrie have the unusual gift to touch many souls and rekindle sad spirits, he also had the remarkable ability to be honest and open. For all of you who never had a professor , teacher, or friend with which you could speak freely, reading this book will make you feel as if Morrie had been your collegue, and all his words and insights were intended for you alone. It is impossible to set this book down because of the amazing insight Morrie had on so many aspects of life. Although the reader knows the final outcome, there is little sadness, because she knows that this man has left behind what few have: an everlasting legacy.

Top stuff TOOL. <br />The only criticism i could have is that TOOL were perhaps trying very hard not to sound like &quot;A PERFECT CIRCLE&quot; [Maynard James Keenans' other band], which when they first appeared, sounded very very similar to early TOOL. But i guess if that was one of their objectives on LATERALUSthey have definitely achieved it.<br />So if you like your hard rock a bit dark and different, give TOOLs LATERALUS a go, and find out why TOOL are regarded as one of the most innovative and unique hard rock bands in the world.

0783225849|5.0|Details, details, details.  It is here where Alfred Hitchcock really towers above other film directors, past or present.  Yes, he was the master of suspense, but so much more.  <br />Of course, this movie has been critiqued and analyzed to death.  Being a huge Hitchcock fan, I guess it is almost embarrassing to say that `Psycho' is probably my favorite - that's so.....unoriginal.  But I have to admit that it is.  And 'Psycho' is a movie that taken as a whole is greater than the sum of it's parts.  It's certainly much more than a terrifying shower murder or a heart-stopping ending scene.<br />In 'Psycho', Hitchcock showcases all of his known and not so well known talents. His manipulative use of the camera.  His careful selection of supporting actors, which gets little mention (Frank Albertson as the rich bank depositor and John Anderson as `California Charlie' come to mind in this movie).  Then there's the way Hitchcock plays with the viewer's mind, controlling his or her emotions at will.  In the scene where Janet Leigh has just stolen the money, is sitting at a stoplight and her boss crosses the street in front of her (this after her telling him she was going home with a headache).....Yikes!  Who hasn't experienced that sudden feeling of panic of being so explicitly caught in a lie or embarrassing act by a boss, a spouse, etc.  It's a perfect Hitchcock moment!<br />Even in his most dark films, Hitchcock can't resist a little satire.  I'm not sure anyone has ever commented on this (although surely someone has), but in the opening scene in the hotel room Janet Leigh is wearing a white bra and slip - after she decides to steal the money and is packing to run away she is wearing black ones.  It took me multiple viewings to pick up on this ( I wonder what other little nuances I haven't noticed yet?).<br />Hitchcock had a `thing' for beautiful women, and was skilled at placing a prominent yet restrained (very restrained by today's standards) sexual emphasis on his female leads.  Tippi Hedren, Grace Kelly, Eva Marie Saint, etc of course were all attractive leads to start.  But Hitchcock's fixation on hair styles, skirt and shoe selection, manner of walk, etc. created a stamp all his own which in my opinion resulted in some of the sexiest women to ever appear on the screen.  Janet Leigh is no exception here (of course I'm not counting the shower scene), whether fully or partially clothed she always looks delicious!  (To other males: Your mileage may vary).<br />Finally, as hinted at earlier, it is sometimes the \\"not so famous\\" scenes in a Hitchcock film which are actually the most memorable.  When Anthony Perkins is waiting for Janet Leigh's car to sink in the marsh, Hitchcock paints the perfect picture of a disturbed mind. As the camera looks up into a moonlit sky we see Anthony Perkin's face, gnawing impatiently on some candy, hands tightly clasped, waiting for his (or should I say his mother's?) victim's auto to finally submerge in the mucky swamp.  Unforgetable - an image that is both twisted yet oddly beautiful.  I know of no other director who wields so much power over our thoughts and feelings.<br />The storyline, cinematography, acting, sub-plots and dialog are all as close to perfection as possible.  It's all here, it's all Hitchcock.  And it will stay with you a lifetime.  Simply one of the best movies ever made.

B000002LE6|5.0|In late 1987, Prince recorded The Black Album, a dark and foreboding record about lust and murder. After he finished making the album, Prince was so disturbed by it that he decided not to release it. Instead, he went back into the studio and recorded a new album called Lovesexy. Lovesexy is the opposite of The Black Album; where The Black Album was dark, violent, and pessemistic, Lovesexy is hopeful, spiritual, and uplifting.<BR><BR> Released in 1988, Lovesexy may be Prince's last great album from beginning to end. The CD is programmed as one long 45-minute track, so that you can't skip around when you listen to it. Prince did this on purpose because he wanted people to listen to the album all the way through. Luckily, the CD is so good that I have no problem playing it all the way through, time and time again. Prince recorded this album with a live band featuring Sheila E. on drums and Boni Boyer on keyboards, among others. The band is amazing, and they add a lot of energy to the album that Prince would not have had on his own. You can hear them singing along and adding their own touches to a lot of the songs, making this album truly a group effort. This is also Prince's most spiritual CD, and, whatever your religion, you will uplifted by this music! <BR><BR> Some standout songs: &quot;Alphabet St.&quot; is a great piece of minimalist funk, with a drum machine and Prince's dry guitar carrying the song nicely. &quot;Glam Slam&quot; is one of Prince's irresistable guitar rock confections, and Sheila E's slammin' drum solo takes the song to a whole new level. &quot;Anna Stesia&quot; is the album's centerpiece and one of Prince's most moving, spiritual ballads ever, right up there with &quot;Purple Rain&quot;! &quot;When 2 R in Love&quot; is another great ballad, adding a dose of romance to the proceedings. &quot;I Wish U Heaven&quot; is truly heavenly, with a melody as smooth and soft as orange sherbet. I feel that this album (and the tour that followed) was the culmination of Prince's SPIRITUAL side, and, if you are feeling down, this CD will definitely make you feel better! A great mix of dance, rock, funk, and passion, with a message of POSITIVITY that will uplift your day.

B00005BL2A|4.0|This album is more along the lines of Depeche Mode's slower moments than Violator's groovier ones.  Exciter is about as un-sellout-ish as Depeche Mode could get.  It's an interesting mix of transitory existential modern European experimental electronica and pre-1970's classic American pop.  This amalgam of sounds builds more successfully on Ultra's less cohesive, less accessible tracks.  There's not much that's similar to their earlier great tracks like Never Let Me Down Again, Stripped, Enjoy The Silence, Judas, and Its No Good.  Comatose and Freelove sound a bit like Ultra, while I Feel Loved is unabashedly more techno.  The Dead of Night leans more towards a harder edge, but still doesn't sound much like their previous best material.  David still hasn't returned to his incredible vocal stylings from the pre-Ultra days, but that's more the fault of his post-rehab vocal coach than himself.  However, one shouldn't miss the group's earlier material since those disks should already be sitting on your CD player.  Standout tracks include Dream On, I Am You, Goodnight Lovers, and Breathe, which has profound lyrics during the second half of the song with its ingenious biblical references.  At times, you can hear some similarities in production to Bjork's Homogenic, which was also produced by Mark Bell.  One or two of the tracks show a slight tendency to mix the music significantly louder than the vocals.  Yet, the overall audio engineering and mastering of the album is incredibly high quality, and much appreciated.  Lovely, patient, and unpretentiously romantic, Exciter deserves our attention and trust as Depeche Mode takes us on this unique sonic and lyrical journey.

0553573403|4.0|I've already ordered George Martin's &quot;A Clash of Kings&quot; and &quot;A Storm of Swords,&quot;  even though the mighty, multi-volume, multiple-viewpoint, medieval-war epic is a genre I usually avoid.<br />I'll continue to read Martin's epic because I'm obsessed with his characters and their stories.<br />Of course, I would rather have fallen in love with his characters (love lasts longer than obsession), but that's hard to do when the author alternates his chapters between eight different  points-of-view (not counting the Prologue).  In spite of his discontinuous narrative, Martin has done a masterful job of orchestrating his characters' fates.  He sweeps his readers up into the lives (one chapter at a time, anyway) of the deposed heirs of the Old Dragon-blood Dynasty, the usurping kings of House Baratheon, and the Northern King-makers of the House Stark.<br />Six Starks dominate the narrative: Lord Eddard of Winterfell, his wife Catelyn, and three of their legitimate children, plus Eddard's bastard son, Jon.<br />Another narrator is Daenerys, daughter of the last Dragon-blood king.  Daenerys manages to escape the destruction of her family, only to be sold into marriage with a character who could pass for Attila the Hun.  She has the most surprising tale to tell, and she is also the character who has to do the most growing and changing in &quot;A Game of Thrones.&quot;  If this were her story alone, Daenerys would be very easy to love, even though she has been given the book's tritest lines:<br /> &quot;My lord,&quot; she said softly.  &quot;Drogo.  My sun-and-stars,&quot; or &quot;I am no child, she told him fiercely.&quot;<br />The final story-teller of the game of thrones is my favorite: Tyrion Lannister, the current Queen's dwarf brother (that's 'dwarf' as in 'short human', not 'dwarf' as in cute, hairy Vernor's gnome).  Tyrion's warrior-brother and sister-Queen are beautiful, golden-haired twins who must have been hell to grow up with, because his verbal sallies and counter-punches are easily the sharpest dialogue in the book.  I always looked forward to reading Tyrion's chapters, and I bet the author enjoyed writing them too.<br />Tyrion is also the book's most complex character.  The adult Starks are very honorable, duty-driven types---easy to admire but a little hard to like.  The numerous child-narrators are likeable, but a bit unformed. Out Of Time also finds the band juggling instruments where drummer Bill Berry plays bass or guitar on some songs, Mr. Mills works alot on keyboards and Mr. Buck tries his hand on the skins.  Out Of Time became the band's first number one album despite the fact that they did not have an extensive tour to support it.  The success was based on the fact that it quite simply was a tremendous album by a band who had clearly grew into their creative peak. B00000JMQC|5.0|Yeah so The Wizard of Oz is a classic. I am a big fan of that film. Frank Baum's brilliant world.<br />Disney could have easily tossed off a sugary sweet rendition of Oz, but director Walter Murch stepped up to the plate and instead produced a wholly unique film: a child's horror movie. OK, its not a typical horror movie - no blood or death or violence. Its tense, its eerie, its unflinching in painting life as hard and some people as bad. Its not REALLY scary if you're over 8 years old, but it is dark, it is disturbing, and it bravely shows how easily dreams and nightmares can evolve from one to the other. <br />A particularly eerie scene, that is effective because of its simplicity, occurs shortly after Dorothy is locked in a hospital. As she stands in a stark white room, on a thin metal bed, the world is as un-magical as any Kansas in MGM's OZ. The difference: the reality here is not just plain, its downright oppressive. While looking out the single dirty window we are suddenly conscious of a the reflection of another entity. As Dorothy turns around she finds a frail young girl, quite pretty, yet abnormally mature and subdued. The camera films her from Dorothy's higher perspective, making her shrink even further into the corners of the room. Her countenance is so strangely adult, and so contrary to her appearance, you know she has to be an apparition. She's frightening, yet immediately we know she means no harm. There is nothing menacing about her sulleness, a truly remarkable combination of character composition and a brilliant rendering of spooky realism - all accomplished with no special effects, no startling sound cue, no threat of violence or gore. Old fashion macabre suspense. Best of all, this is done by two honest, focused, natural young women, and a director who obviously respects and trusts young people.<br />Other horrors include the wheelers (again showing how easy it is to achieve the effect you want with talented performers rather than expensive computers) and the delightfully cruel witch Mombi, played to perfection by Jean Marsh. <br />Fairuza Balk is good, simply because she lacks all that annoying child actor cuteness. She's never 'clever' or 'bratty' or 'heroic' or 'cloying'. She simply reacts to what happens to her. Nothing more, nothing less. Her Dorothy is a curious and self sufficient little girl, but still a little girl. Its refreshing to see a young actor simply 'be' on screen. <br />People who hate this movie probably don't care for fantasy. If you like the genre, and if you like it a little on the dark side, then you will probably like this film. If you still relish in make-believe, see this film. If you are capable of re-imagining your surroundings, see this film. If you recognize and face life's most ugly characters and come away both stronger and more compassionate for it, see this film.<br />If you want every movie to have dancing munchkins... well... I'm sure you know what to watch.

6305342776|4.0|A caveat before I begin.  The Black Hole came out when I was four years old.  For the next few years, I had Black Hole wallpaper in my bedroom.  Until it got too beat up for use, I had a Black Hole lunchbox and sitting in my old desk in my parent's house are my Vincent and Maxmillian toys that somehow have survived from 1979.  <br />So, suffice to say, &quot;The Black Hole&quot; was a big part of my childhood.  Having seen the film a number of times, and purchasing it on vhs upon rerelease a couple of years ago, I came to the revelation that it was (at best) an average sci-fi film.  The four-star rating derives from gratitude more than anything else.  Some aspects of the film do stand out.  <br />One of the notable achievements of the film is John Barry's score, which in one-third brooding, one-third hyperkinetic, and one-third heroic march.  In a way, the great variance in Barry's scoring highlights the fundamental flaw of &quot;The Black Hole&quot;.  To use a cliche, the plot is painted with wide strokes and alternates between a number of science fiction film norms, never staying for too long in any one type of groove.  At moments, the film plays like a vintage '50's morality tale a la &quot;Day the Earth Stood Still&quot; or &quot;Forbidden Planet&quot;.  Just as quickly, the film becomes a &quot;Star Wars&quot; redux with the heroes in white facing off against faceless villains.  Finally, in small portions through and dominating at the end, there are streaks of existentialist pretention that would have made Kubrick blush (or at least feel flattered).  <br />In short, the film itself never amounts to anything too deep because it never develops one theme or plays on one tone for too long for anything to come of it.  However, the film still gets a four-star rating.  Partially on nostalga, yes, but like so many films that transcend their plot loopholes and work, &quot;The Black Hole&quot; has a number of positives going for it.<br />As is often the case with many cult films or popcorn movies, &quot;The Black Hole&quot; has a distinguished cast of actors who lend the film more weight than it ought to have.  The special effects also give &quot;The Black Hole&quot; an intensity that carrys the audience through the film.  In fact, even by todays standards, the set design and FX execution hold up remarkably well.  Whereas the thematic tone of the film never seems to ring true or consistently, the mood of the film is carried and maintained by the sets and special effects.  In short, the film is an enjoyable romp on the sum of its parts if not its whole.<br />Ultimately, though, I imagine this film will fall into the same hole as other films (to use Disney as an example) such as &quot;Pete's Dragon&quot; or &quot;Old Yeller&quot; that will remain special to those who saw it in their youth, but be lost to newer generations of film watchers with countless more current popcorn genre flicks to choose from.  Alas.<br />Still, for any fan of science fiction and any fan of a good popcorn film, don't be afraid to get sucked into &quot;The Black Hole&quot;.  <br />And, when you go, please take that terrible pun with you.

B00005B36H|5.0|It's been a long wait, but Tool has made the five years well worth it.<br />They take all the best things about their 1996 masterpiece Aenima and run with them, extending and stretching the genre much further.  In fact, Lateralus is more of a step from Aenima than Aenima was from Undertow in terms of weaving intricate time signatures, complex and shifting syncopations and emphases, and starkly contrasting dynamics and tones.  There is no other band on the planet doing what Tool is doing.<br />To the new Tool listener: This music is as demanding on the listener as it was on the band to write and perform. Layers of complex rhythm and sound swirl around in a chaotic miasma, waiting for you to discover something new every time. I still hear new things almost every time I listen to Aenima. If you're not up to the challenge, or are looking for another Perfect Circle album, don't say I didn't warn you.<br />To my fellow musicians: You can't afford NOT to be listening to this album. Each of the four members is one of the best around at his instrument (and I consider Maynard's voice a beautiful instrument the way he uses it), and yet the four individuals put all selfishness aside in the interests of creating great music as a group. I am especially blown away by the increased presence and complexity of Justin's bass playing. The bass, guitar and drums serve as separate voices, which explore different territory at times, yet ultimately work together to become something more than the sum of the parts.<br />In the context of the history of progressive rock, this is absolutely a new milestone. From the great 70's prog bands like Rush and Yes to the 80's prog masters Voivod (ALL bassists and guitarists should own the albums &quot;Dimension Hatross&quot; and &quot;Nothingface&quot;), Tool has assumed the role of leader of the genre in the last 8 years. In terms of technical musical complexity, I dare say (after having only owned it for 24 hours) that this is one of the most advanced recordings ever made. And the amazing thing is, at the same time, Tool maintains the level of emotional intensity of their previous work while delivering this amazing technical and well-polished performance.<br />To prove my point: My brother, who is an honors doctoral student in music theory at a major university, wrote as part of his Master's thesis an analysis of Tool's song &quot;Third Eye&quot; (the magnum opus of Aenima). He is doing a 3-hour graduate-level independent study of Lateralus in the fall.<br />Lateralus should prove a hugely rewarding listen to those who actively particiapate in the experience with the band.<br />Favorites after only three listens: &quot;The Grudge&quot;, &quot;Reflection&quot;, &quot;The Patient&quot;, and &quot;Lateralis&quot;. (Notice it's spelled differently from the album title, just as track 13 on Aenima was called &quot;Aenema&quot; and had a very different meaning).

1563890119|5.0|Preludes and Nocturns is my favorite book period.  I write this review in order to 1)make my case and 2) allow my case to influence the overall star rating of this work.          Preludes begins with the explorations of an occult society in modern day England, an obvious comment on the place that the mystical has been confined too in the modern world.  What a better figure for what Gaimen is doing?  Gaimen begins his journey from the fantastically real (traditional modern comic) to the fantastical (Sandman comics) in mundane (if you consider occult rituals and the like mundane, which in the comic world, it is) reality.  Admitedly, the 'sleeping disease' was a little bit weak, but beautifully done.        From here, Gaimen establishes Morpheous as a character by having him, esseintally, reconstruct himself.  This has become such a pervasive theme in recent theatrical and literary works, that the only comment worth making here is that Gaimen did it before it was cool.        Further, can you have a more visceral conveyence of the reality and delusions of a madman than '24 Hours'?  I think not. Anyone who is not transfixed and horrified by that episode should return to thier fufilling lives of Ally McBeal and reruns of the Cosby Show.          Finally, 'The Flutter of Her Wings' provides a welcome and necessary contrast to the horror of '24 Hours' and demonstrates Gaimens perceptive need for emotional juxtaposition even when so allegedly 'inexperienced'.  Without this episode, while in my opinon certainly not the most affective episode, the graphic novel would be incomplete, unbalanced.  Beyond that, the addition of anymore episodes of this tone would have abraded the feeling of release that 'Her Wings' presented.        Preludes and Nocturns is my favorite work in the Sandman series, for perhaps the very reason that the narrative is not so meticously constructed.  Gaimen was, at this point, playing with his ideas, and it lent a tone of possibility and mysticism that resonated throughout the rest of the series, as the series became more and more routinized in traditional plot structure.  In my opinion, Preludes is the work that broke the ground for Sandman, not as a well-written series, but as a well done and well-exicuted series.

Dune follows a complex plot, that although science fiction, capitvates the reader with extreme reality.  It's been a long time since I've read a book - fantasy, science fiction, or otherwise - that relays such a believable message to the ever avid reader.  But not ony the complexity of the plot attacted me to this novel.  Dune is masterfully written.  There's no other way to put it.  The characters are so devoloped that the reader feels for them, hates them, and understands them. I don't recommend it for children - some of the topics, though perfectly appropriate, are extremely hard to grasp. If you want to pick up this book, be ready to re-read a few sections, especially if you have trouble understanding the words between the lines.<br />Thanks for staying with me for so long! Those who skipped to the end, I'll sum it all up here: Dune's complexity makes it strikingly real and believavle, yet often times strict patience is required - as in real life.  But if you're wondering whether to buy it - I didn't give it five stars for nothing.  It's worth every penny, and more.

0201633612|5.0|Design Patterns, referred to warmly as the &quot;Gang of Four&quot; book by it's fans, is the book that introduced me to the world of design patterns.  After reading this book, you'll immediately see patterns from it everywhere - many classes you use every day will suddenly all fit together in a pattern.<br />Besides being a tool for writing good maintainable code, patterns are a crucial tool for communication among developers.  Knowing even a half-dozen of the basic patterns will facilitate communication among team members immensely.  For instance, you may be having a whiteboard design session with another developer or two, explaining some particular design you have in your head.  Instead of having to detail 3 or 4 or more classes and how they would interact, you could simply say that you are thinking of using the Factory pattern here, and the Decorator pattern over there, and the others will know exactly what you are thinking.<br />The patterns are grouped into three groups: Creational, Structual, and Behavioral patterns.  Each pattern is considered in turn, each having sections detailing the intent of the pattern, the motivation for using it, the consequences (both good and bad) of its application, collaboration among the objects involved, and examples.<br />The examples are mostly in C++ with a small dose of Smalltalk, but the patterns are equally applicable to any object-oriented language, Java in particular.  Some of the patterns can be implemented even more simply and cleanly in Java, as a result of language features such as dynamic class loading, among others.<br />This book is well-written, and it's a quality book well worth owning.  It even has two nice ribbon bookmarks attached to the binding which makes to book even more practical as well as handsome.

B00000JJQK|5.0|Back in the summer of '74 as I was preparing to enter the 9th grade, I picked up the Beach Boys &quot;Endless Summer&quot;, and as an after thought, a copy of &quot;Pet Sounds&quot; (wasn't it all surf music?). As much as I enjoyed songs like &quot;I get Around&quot; and &quot;Surfer Girl&quot;, it was Pet Sounds that I kept coming back to as a pimply faced teen. The darn album spoke to me. The youthful innocence of falling in love. The pain of falling out of love. Then there was the sound. My god, that sound. Pianos being plucked from the inside. Bicycle horns and bells to signify innocence. Kettle drums playing a melody. Astonishing vocal arrangements. An entire song (&quot;Let's go away for a while&quot;) that is impossible to humm or whistle the melody to... I felt like I accidently discovered a Mona Lisa in the attic. It was one of those rare albums where you could close the bedroom door to the world and get totally lost in the music - and hear something different with each listening. Why wasn't this universally loved back in '66?. Thirty-five years after it was born, it's finally getting its due by more people than the critics. There has never been quite another album like it. In some respects it's like Thomas Hart Benton meets George Gershwin, and it's as current now as it was back then. In the age of synths and overdubs, the sound on this album will not likely be reproduced. Recommended for anybody who is, hopes to, or ever was in love, and for people with ears. It's a sonic masterpiece. Sir George McCartney summed it up best when he said &quot;nobody's musical education is complete without this album&quot;. Not only has Mojo dubbed this the greatest album of all-time, but so has Britain's NME (New Music Express). I can't argue.

0767816641|5.0|I don't watch many movies, and have recently lost my taste for kids' movies in particular--which are also the few movies which don't have gratuitous sex, violence, and cursing. This movie manages to transcend genres, a witty film that those who have outgrown the \\"Madeleine\\" cartoons will enjoy.<br />Madeleine, played by the excellent Hatty Jones, is a little French girl at a boarding school in France, along with eleven other girls (also flawlessly acted) and their teacher Miss Clavel (Frances McDormand). The movie follows her clashes with the Spanish ambassador's son, Pepito, and with Lord \\"Cuckooface\\" Covington, the man who owns the school upon the death of their sponsor--and who wants to close it down; also with such problems as a ransom plot, and the loyal dog Genevieve.<br />Madeleine purists should be warned: this movie is not just a live-action version of the book stories. The movie exists as a series of vignettes loosely based on the books, such as Madeleine's appendectomy and her rescue by Genevieve. Two of the central plots are not in the books, however: Lord Covington's wish to sell the school, and the kidnapping plot concerning Pepito. Occasionally dialogue will be lifted (a difficulty, given the almost complete lack of REAL dialogue) such as Miss Clavel's midnight \\"Something is not right\\" or Madeleine's defiant \\"pooh pooh!\\"<br />The actors and script contain the necessary amount of innocent cuteness and comedy (the visits of the various ambassadors to the school) while also having poignancy. One thing that will irritate Madeleine purists is Madeleine's family situation in this movie, but it does give her the ability to interact with Lord Covington.<br />McDormand is excellent as the rather put-upon nun Miss Clavel, who stiffly glides through real life while trying to keep her twelve little girls in something approaching order without squelching them. Thank God the old \\"authority figure as clown\\" thing never came into play. Hatty Jones is cute and sprightly without being annoyingly so, while the actresses for such characters as Vicki and Chantal are perfect in their roles. The girls REGISTER as being real people. So does Kristian de la Osa, as the attention-hungry Pepito--the movie hints quietly at why he is such a \\"bad hat,\\" while simultaneously giving us sympathy for him and Madeleine alike. And kudos to the best original character, Helene the cook!<br />Music is overall very good (thankfully no one breaks into spontaneous song) with sedate music playing for Miss Clavel and the girls, sad music during poignant moments, and excellent Spanish numbers as we focus on Pepito. The final number stinks, though, it clashes with the setting and plotline.<br />The minimal violence is cartoonish and there is no innuendo; one (bad word) creeps into the script, via a bad guy.<br />This movie is a delight from start to finish, a witty and charming movie that will take you back to your childhood. B00005B36H|5.0|Tool once again produces a CD that not only brings us the best of what we love from them, but also is amazingly fresh.  Hard, considering Tool's sound is one-of-a-kind...the mix of the trashing guitar, pumping bass and thumping guitars along with Keenan's voice that may jump from whispering tremble to scream in seconds is not the kind of style that becomes more original with each new album.  Yet Tool proves its worth as a band by making each album more original and smothered in truth and power.  With Undertow they brought us rock, and with Aenema they threw in a little more art, now with Lateralus we find Tool back-to-the-wall.  Five years after Aenema, and fresh-off Maynard's stint with a more harmonious, angelic-sounding Perfect Circle this album came with high expectations...but of what?<br />It's still Tool and that's for sure, there are hints of A Perfect Circle, Keenan's cascading vocals being one of them, and yet the music and mood are constantly dark and engulfed in ambience.  Tool has created what is such a great cd because it is just what they are: art.  Not to be taken in small doses, Lateralus runs a lengthy 78 minutes.  Songs have sequels, songs changes harmony so quickly that one might check to see if the track switched.  It's meant to be heard as one, not singles.  It isn't a pop album in any sense of the word, it's an attack on the senses.  Songs cascade with so many sounds it takes a minute or two to find order in the chaos, once it makes sense and filters itself out, the music is dumbfoundingly good.  Tool has created a record beyond expectations and certainly original.<br />Finding out, next, that Maynard's lyrical compositions had grown as well as the music proved that this cd was special.  Still comes the poetical depression, and broken-down aggression, but gone are all useless f---s, and brilliant lines and intelligent thoughts replace them.  Instead of listing and [doing otherwise with the] things he hates as he did Aenema, this time around Maynard (in Ticks and Leeches) takes his anger out on lawyers using the metaphor parasites sucking at him.  \\"I hope you're choking / I hope you choke on this\\" he half-wails, half-screams.  It's not as if he's given up, but accepted what will be and takes it upon himself to make a point of yelling about it.<br />Lateralus may be Tool's best work yet.  From craftmanship to lyrics to raw emotion they bring what we expect and more.  The best metal album since...well...Aenima.

B00005B36H|5.0|I think that it's safe to say that Tool's definately topped themselves with Lateralus. Even the psychedelic packaging shows that this is an album unlike any other. The thing is that it's hard to judge this album just by hearing the single Schism. Tool meant it to be that way. You'll never be able to judge any Tool album - past, present, or future just on the strength of its singles. The singles fit into the album like a jigsaw puzzle, and it's really difficult to judge with just one piece. <br />Lateralus has quite a few elements from AEnima, you have the songs that top 7, 8, and even 11 minutes. Not for the impatient listener, by any means. Spliced in between are short instrumentals, designed as mere interludes between one song and the next. Upon first listen, Reflection will remind you of AEnima's longer anthems, like Push It and Third Eye, however, it's not quite as intense as the latter. It's a lot more quiet than Third Eye was, without all the heavy textures, sound samples, and such. There's no straight-forward metal numbers like Stinkfist, though Schism does come off as one. This one requires serious listening. There really is no bad song on this CD, since it's meant to be listened to as a whole in one sitting. The true standouts are Triad, a long Hendrix or Zepplin-esque guitar solos and heavily textured backgrounds. Also, Reflection, Parabol/Parabola, and the title song merit mention as being the standouts on this modern rock masterpiece.  While AEnima was a very intense album, this ups the intensity factor 10 fold. The best way to enjoy this album - close the windows and doors, turn the lights off, close your eyes and crank it up to 11. Only then will you be able to appreciate Maynard's true insane genius. This CD is damn near perfect, and all Tool, modern rock, whatever fans need to have this CD in their collections. Lateralus will cement its place as as a classic for years to come. It was definately worth the wait.

B00005BL2A|3.0|I'll cut to the chase here - Exciter is not a blockbuster album in any sense of the word.  We'll start at the beginning.<br />There are no standout tracks on the album, so it takes a few listens for the music to start to sink in.  All the tracks here have a laid-back, atmospheric feel to them, so don't expect any poppy dance songs, moody epics, or straight-out rockers like on previous discs.<br />The music is quite different from any other Depeche Mode album - things are sparse, and there are plenty of samples to go around.  I suppose you could call it &quot;minimal electronic&quot; if you want.  This is especially evident on the first single, &quot;Dream On&quot; - an acoustic guitar, some ambient samples, and a skittering drum beat.  Dave Gahan's vocals are better than anything else he's done to this point, and Martin Gore turns in his usual fantastic performances on &quot;Comatose&quot; and &quot;Breathe&quot;.<br />My real problem is with the lyrics, which come off as uninspired and uninteresting.  In the worst cases, they're downright embarassing to listen to, such as the chorus to &quot;The Dead Of Night&quot; (we are the dead of night/we're in the zombie room/we're twilight's parasites/with self-inflicted wounds).  The other lyrics focus on love, life, the usual topics, but while some phrases seem like they should have an impact, nothing really clicks.<br />It's not a bad album, but it's not as varied as Black Celebration or as dynamic as Violator.  I'll have to recommend this for Depeche Mode fans only.

Lucifer Sam, Flaming and Gnome are fairly typical psychadelic tunes of the period. Pow R. Toc H. is another of the improvised jazz numbers which characterised early Floyd jam-sessions. The truncated and obscure variation of Interstellar Overdrive must have been for the benefit or regular UFO Club visitors since the full-length original had not yet been released on record. (Officially, that is). The influence of Dave Brubeck on both tracks is clear.<br />Mathilda Mother, Chapter 24 and Scarecrow belong to what would today be described as 'new age' music. As such they predate the folk-rock boom of the late 1960's/early 1970's in England and demonstrate just how far Syd Barrett's compositons were ahead of their time. Roger Waters has stated elsewhere ... that 'Saucerful Of Secrets' demonstrates why Barrett had to leave the band. I would say that Bike - inspired as far as I know by the movie adaptation of Martin Amis' Here We Go Round The Mulberry Bush - already forsignalled the inevitable split from the band. It is brilliant, it is artisitic but whether it is musical is highly subjective. Take Up Thy Stethoscope is a raw example of Waters' own early compostition.<br />Despite its obvious shortcomings, Piper remains an essential purchase for anyone with an interest in the early development of either the group or Syd Barrett.

B00005B36H|5.0|Finally. After five long years of waiting, one of the 2001's most highly anticipated albums has been released. Tool's Lateralus, the follow up to 1996's highly successful AEnima, is more magnificent of an album than I ever could have hoped for. Even someone as completely skeptical and angst-filled as I, can do nothing but sit in awe of the breath-taking sounds that emanate from my stereo's speakers as I type this CD's praises. With only thirteen tracks, the album clocks in at an impressive 80 minutes. The songs are more like opuses; the lyrics are poetic, and the musicianship is excellent. The parts are complex, but as well arranged as any orchestral piece. They also rest on that fine line of being musical and heavy at the same time.  One of the main highlights is Maynard James Keenan's trademark powerful yet introverted vocal performances. Though they are not as ballad-esque and haunting as those of his side project, A Perfect Circle, this album better demonstrates his vocal abilities than any of Tool's previous releases. Another highpoint is Danny Carey's almost inhuman drumming. His pounding, tribal beats add such a building intensity to the songs that it's easy to forget that they're being created by a mere human armed with only two arms, two legs, and a pair of drumsticks. All the band members are masters of their craft, seeming more like four solo artists who happen to play together than a typical band.  All these elements combined give Tool and this album a sound and a quality that puts them well ahead of many of the current metal bands. On a scale of one to ten, I would have to give an 11.5861. This album has the ability to make one lose themselves in the music and have an out-of-body experience without the use of other harmful, illegal stimulants. For Tool fans, and music fans in general, this album was definitely worth the wait.

0452282152|3.0|Ignoring scholarly confusion about the identity of the sitter for this famous painting, Chevalier invents a housemaid whose intelligence and resourcefulness come into conflict with the meekness expected of those in her station. Perhaps it was the psychologically complex combination of uncertainty and surprise in the portrait that inspired her. The beautiful girl, dressed like neither a lady nor a maid, seems ill at ease and willful at the same time. The novel is a tale of domestic intrigue in 17th-century Delft with a minimum of details bringing the time and place to life. Delft never really rises physically before our eyes, unfortunately, and Vermeer's luminous paintings are described as shockingly mundane lists of the objects and people they depict, without a glimmer of the dialogue between silence and light that is what makes them extraordinary. The closest Chevalier comes is in her description of the figure in another painting: &quot;A window lit her from the left, falling across her face and tracing the delicate curve of her forehead and nose.&quot; (A window fell across her face?) Nothing in the writing hints at why these works of art are special, beyond the fact that everyone says they are. Chevalier isn't really interested in the paintings, or in Vermeer, who is little more than a cipher. Her subject is particular: the imagined story behind a painting. In unadorned language, suitable to her protagonist, she creates a cautious, observant, thoughtful young woman the reader comes to both know and like. Most of the other characters fail to emerge as vivid individuals, with the exception of a senior maid, Tanneke. There are murky references to a possible attraction between the master and the maid which should have been deleted -- Griet, the girl with a pearl earring, rightly concludes that Vermeer was always more interested in the painting than the sitter. Chevalier, the young novelist, should have learned from Griet's wisdom. The novel's flaws notwithstanding, it's an enjoyable read, and Griet's lot in life after she sits for the fateful painting has a satisfying authenticity.

0790734060|5.0|This film is a masterpiece both of the cinema and of music. Mozart is the archetype of the composer and musician who transforms, every so often, the field of music by going beyond all canons and rules, or even regulations. He is the archetype of any creative person, in any field, who needs freedom to invent new vistas and perspectives, who needs tolerance and goodwill to be able to present to society what he is creating, to bring to the market and the public what he is inventing. Mozart is like a God of our western civilization.<br /><br />The film shows how, in his case, Mozart cannot dissociate his general provocative attitude from his creativity, how his best tunes, his best pieces of music come from the people he meets, the people he loves, the people who are hostile and try to crush him into death, and will eventually succeed by forcing him into composing his own Requiem.<br /><br />The first one in this line is his father who made him a genius and then treated him like a dog who has to obey all his finger-wagging and all his eye-frowning. The second is the Emepror who is both narrow-minded due to his vanity, and tolerant but not as much as necessary, just enough to sleep well at night. He tolerates an innovation and then kills it with a yawn that foretells about the peaceful night he is going to have. The third one is the official composer of the court who will do all he can to destroy this young man who is pushing him into oblivion and non-existence. He is jealous, angry, criminal, and he will dictate Mozart's death while being dictated the Requiem directly by Mozart from his deathbed. The fourth one is his mother in law who manipulates her daughter into going away with Mozart's son at the very moment when he needed both his wife and his son most.<br /><br />Then we could speak of the public who is manipulated into liking what is supposed to be liked and is decreed to be liked by the Emepror. This public is a caricature of taste and artistic feeling. Luckily there is another public, the popular public, who is responsive to innovation, to beauty, to entertainment, to anything that is new and changes their way of looking at things.<br /><br />But this film is also a phenomenal Requiem to Mozart who will be buried in a hemp-sack, in a common grave for the poor. The greatest genius in music, the greatest composer who has changed so many things in the tradition he was inheriting and has at the same time created modern music, is discarded by good society, is crushed by the status of pure slave that composers have to accept and free composers can't, is destroyed into death by a society that is not ready to accept that they will eventually die and that they must accept new forms of expression and expressivity for the sake of the next and coming generations. B000007MVX|5.0|There are few groups who attempt to make art from the humble beginnings of rock music - Jethro Tull are one such group.  &quot;A Passion Play&quot; is not the sort of album you'll want to put on as background music at your next dinner party, but albums for that kind of occasion are a dime-a-dozen.  This is music that demands the fullness of one's attention, appealing to the intellect while also presenting moments of immediacy that just whisk the listener along.<br />&quot;A Passion Play&quot; is one forty-five minute song (though CD reissues have added names to each segment, you can still put it on and let it play as one, continuous piece).  It is a song of many parts, many movements and emotions, telling the allegorical story of an individual's life after death,  with a reflection on life being its primary lyrical concern (&quot;I'd give up my halo for a horn, and the horn for the hat I once had&quot;).  Musically, it sees Tull leader Ian Anderson expanding his virtuosity beyond flute and acoustic guitar to a variety of exotic saxophones, and his oh-so-British vocals have a range and suppleness rarely matched on other albums.  Keyboardist John Evans' early 1970s synthesizers are also particularly worthy of praise.  <br />There are melodic acoustic passages that are just gorgeous, and driving riff-rock, and a multi-tracked flute solo that is spellbinding.  So many moods and feelings forming a cohesive whole, which is quite appropriate considering its subject matter!  But, just to prevent the whole thing from being viewed as too &quot;pretentious,&quot; the surreal &quot;Story of the Hare Who Lost His Spectacles&quot; is presented as a sort of &quot;Intermission&quot; to &quot;A Passion Play,&quot; and it is quite difficult to describe!<br />Some consider &quot;Aqualung&quot; to be Tull's definitive work.   &quot;A Passion Play&quot; comes much closer to the mark.   So, turn out the lights, turn on the stereo and dig in!

B00000GAIW|3.0|Jethro Tull's fourth album, made in 1971, has long been held up as the band's definitive work.  While many of the songs are wonderful, complex, and layered with the electric/acoustic tensions that mark Jethro Tull's best work, there are far too many moments that fall flat, mostly due to odd or even seemingly poor production.  There are much more consistent Tull albums.<br />There is a crispness and vitality missing here that is present to this day when Jethro Tull play these songs live, which reinforces the feeling that the production itself is at fault.  Ian Anderson's flute is stunning and well to the fore, but his vocals (particularly on the &quot;heavy' rock numbers where his snarling delivery is meant to accentuate the weighty themes of his lyrics) are flat and abrasive.  His softer delivery on the acoustic tracks works much better in the context of the album as a whole.<br />This album is worth owning, though, for &quot;My God,&quot; one of Ian Anderson's finest compositions.  It encapsulates everything that makes Tull special  - being part acoustic number, part challenging progressive rock piece,  and featuring a tour-de-force flute solo from Mr. Anderson.  <br />This is one good album in the midst of the Jethro Tull's vast, wonderful output, but hardly their definitive statement.  For that, check out &quot;A Passion Play&quot; or &quot;Roots To Branches...&quot;

B00005B36H|5.0|Tool have always been groundbreaking in their fusion of progressive, metal, and abstract to create a sonic experience that while heavy and sometimes disturbing is always a musical experience not to be forgotten. Danny Carey's drums are as brutual as ever on &quot;Lateralus,&quot; but takes a strange tribal direction on certain songs, giving the album the feeling of isolated wilderness from which the listener might not return. And finally...Maynard James Keenan, the legend in his own time...his voice blisters and burns with passion, moreso than on any other Tool album, or his work with A Perfect Circle. Tool as a band have a beautiful chemistry that puts other bands to shame, both in the interplay between the members...and of course in the music. Songs on this album that help to put &quot;Lateralus&quot; ahead of &quot;Undertow,&quot; &quot;Aenima,&quot; or &quot;Opiate.&quot; Don't get me wrong, I'd still listen to the earlier albums, but &quot;Lateralus&quot; has to be their tightest and most brilliant album. B00004SU5J|4.0|St. Germain do for jazz what Portishead did for the torch song - take a now-marginal, nostalgia-muffled form and update it with glacial modern production, while retaining the potent pleasures of the old form.  the first five tracks of 'Tourist' are sheer bliss: the straight, allusive bop rush of 'Rose Rouge'; the Wes Montgomery-strum to a languid trip hop backing of 'Montego Bay Spleen'.  'So Flute' is actually so piano, that instrument extemporising thrillingly; as are the next two - the supposedly trumpet-driven 'Land of...' is usurped by a gorgeous melodic epiphany, while the xylophone reverie of 'Latin Note' bows to the staccato.<br />No album could sustain that level of dreamy excitement, and there is a drop.  It's not that the music's bad: 'Sure Thing' is a dirty great blues beast, while 'La Goutte D'Or' is that rare thing, disco-jazz; but the slackening of discipline leads to occasional attention-wandering.

There are some scenes in the film that are just disturbing: the prison riot, and Sizemore's killing of a prostitute come to mind. So this movie is not for the faint of heart. But for everyone else, it is a must-see satire of the American justice system and how the media glorifies violence.  Overall, a haunting, yet IMMENSELY satisfying movie.

Adam Jones simply never ceases to amaze with his skill on guitar. When you buy this album, go to a quiet place with your CD player.  Lay down comfortably, close your eyes and hit play.  Don't move until the end of track 13.  Let the music take your mind on a journey.  This is Tool.

B000002UAR|5.0|Revolver is the Beatles most balanced and complete album in terms of pure songwriting quality and diversity--it should be the first studio album recommended for anyone who has never heard a Beatles album. Rubber Soul has fantastic stuff, but has &quot;filler&quot; (in the Beatles own context of course). Paul's obligatory pretty love song &quot;Michelle&quot; is fluff (Dylan made a snide comment to Lennon about the latter song) without even comparing it to the stunning &quot;Here, there and Everywhere&quot; on &quot;Revolver&quot;. &quot;What Goes On&quot; is a Carl Perkinish throwaway for Ringo to sing. &quot;Think for Yourself&quot; is weak and &quot;The Word&quot; is a tepid, prototype hippie love song far surpassed by later songs in the same theme. &quot;Run For Your Life&quot; (&quot;I'd rather see you dead little girl than see you with another man&quot;?!) is pure throwaway--Lennon himself admitted he was uncomfortable with the lyric and that the song was filler written in the vein of Elvis Rockabilly. This proves that the Beatles were beyond all that by late '65 and didn't need to attempt so closely imitate their musical mentors. Of course, when I say &quot;filler&quot; I mean in the context of the Beatles complete catalog--I would rather listen to Beatles &quot;filler&quot; than many other artists &quot;best of&quot; collections. &quot;Revolver&quot; is pure musical muscle--no fat (except perhaps George's &quot;Love You Too&quot;. Of his &quot;Indian&quot; trilogy songs, &quot;The Inner Light&quot; is by far George's best, unfortunately, as the B-side to &quot;Hey Jude&quot; it was hardly ever heard and never released on a studio album--you can find it on the singles collection &quot;Past Masters, vol II&quot;). Its too bad the fantastic singles released as an &quot;appetizer&quot; for this album (&quot;Paperback Writer&quot; &amp; &quot;Rain&quot;) aren't on the album as well (the Beatles routinely released singles for radio airplay to &quot;preview&quot; an album without the singles even being included on the album itself). Remove the summer of love psychedelia and commercial hype from Pepper and it stands a bit weak compared to &quot;Revolver.&quot; Even Pepper's famous &quot;A Day in the Life&quot; is given a good run for its money by Revolver's &quot;Tommorow Never Knows&quot;--both moody psychedlic masterpieces by John that end each album. Again, only the Beatles can compete with themselves--they invented their own category. this is the studio album to put in the hand of the novice.

B00004X01U|4.0|For anyone interested in Madonna's Music CD, the singles you've probably heard on the radio - Music, Don't Tell Me, What It Feels Like For a Girl - are strong indicators of  what this album is about. Lots of catchy hooks draped over sparse, jagged, electro backdrops are pretty much is the gist of this whole CD.<br />The album is a surefire success in its intent to mesh the old in with the new - to take the Madonna of the 80's the world fell in love with, and give her a sonic makeover (not that she necessarily needed one from her fantastic 1998 release). The first three tracks really conjure up unforgettable hooks that really seem to connect with the heart of what Madonna's old music used to draw from, her spunky delivery, the catch  phrases, etc. while at the same time titillate the listener with all the perks of modern sound technology.<br />The production is highly experimental, and overall, it works. The production is pretty much the highlight of the album, because most of its real artistic merit is the results of producers honing their blitzes and bleeps in a way that  complements the song. The title track best exemplifies this, because it would take a litany of descriptions here to  describe that track accurately to someone who hasn't heard it. Production aside, there really is not much of any &quot;artistic&quot; value on the CD, as the harmonic songwriting is static and banal (unlike many of her masterpieces with her longtime collaborator Pat Leonard, such as Live to Tell, Like a Prayer, and Frozen), and the lyrics are alot more mainstream and a bit trite/banal, presenting Madonna mostly in a way that would appeal to the least common denominator among listeners. Madonna's always had a way of taking a really common line and making it seem like it really means something. That's been a great hallmark of her career, but somehow, it seems to be occasionally faltering  here, despite the use of more lofty words (makes the bourgeoise wanna rebel?), which ironically seem a bit forced as though she were trying to affirm her intellect to the listener.  Smart as she may be, she sounds alot better and more natural saying &quot;I Wanna Dance With My Baby&quot; than arbitrary stuff like &quot;Astral Bodies Drip Like Wine.&quot; So the word choice works in some areas, but can be quite hit and miss. Also her narrative shift since her last release is a bit disappointing; gone is the mystique and mature, in with the fresh and the funk. Not necessarily a &quot;bad&quot; thing objectively, although I have a personal preference for the former.<br />After the first three tracks, the album goes off on this cowboy-country-hiphop tangent... I applaud her for trying it, and it sounds pretty good overall.<br />Soundwise, it's a step forward in terms of production and edginess (except for a few songs by one producer that were actually just stripped apart to sound more like another's - and that compromise rather vitiated those songs, Runaway Lover, Amazing, and Gone) and the hearken back to her olden days and carefree style is a huge plus. It's Madonna in fine form. Assuming that was her objective, it succeeds brilliantly.<br />The problem is that... it depends what you as a customer are looking for. Her previous effort, Ray of Light, was such an evolution for her narrative persona and delivery, with pinnacles of emotion and engagement, all executed with such pitch-perfect intimacy, that it really makes me scratch my head to wonder why she would take this more commercialesque direction rather than go forward.<br />Thus, I'm just imagining that intimacy was not the intent here, and that would explain its absence.<br />So the quality of this CD really depends on what kind of mood your in. If you're looking for profound, you won't find it here. On the other hand, if you're looking for funky, fun, and some generally groovy urban tunes, this album's for you.

In fact, 'Document' is merely typical of many a good album which should have been great; it starts well, picks up pace in the middle, but falls away disappointingly towards the end.  To my ears, 'Document' is a flawed album, its reputation forged merely by the nostalgic turns of 'It's The End Of The World' and 'The One I Love'. Don't be fooled.
