The power struggle is centered on a need for a substance called \\"the spice\\" and involves several parties, including the Imperium, the Harkonnens, the Atreides and the Guild.  This power struggle sends the main character, Paul Atreides, into the deep southern Arrakeen desert where he has to learn how to live on a dry, worm infested, desert.  Dune demonstrates the idea that humans must adapt to their environments in order to survive.   One of the earlier examples of adaptation is the use of still suits.  Still suits are specially designed garments, made by Fremen (natives of Arrakis), which prevent the loss of bodily-water.  These garments are essential in the deep desert because there are no water sources.  Paul and his mother, Jessica, have never before used still suits on their home planet, Caladan, so they had to adapt to the desert and learn to use them.  Without the still suits, the two would not have survived their escape to the south, and could not have found the Fremen.  It is the Harkonnen's lack of quality still suits that kept them from exploring the deep desert.  This adaptation is very essential to the plot because there would have been no story if they hadn't found the Fremen; Paul and Jessica would be dead. Paul had to learn the Fremen ways.  Like how land, air and sea power are utilized on Earth, the Fremen utilize \\"desert power\\" on Arrakis.  Desert power is more important that anything else on Arrakis because of the simple fact that Arrakis is all desert.  Desert power involves the effective use of all desert resources; using worms for the water of life and transportation, sandstorms to knock out shields on an assault, and spice to enhance the mind.  The Fremen taught Paul their religion, and all of the aspects of desert power.  If he had not adapted to the desert by learning the Fremen ways, Paul would not have learned that the spice opened up his revelations and he would not have been able to mount the successful final assault on the Imperial city.  Neither the Harkonnens nor the Sardaukar (Imperial forces) had desert power so they were massacred (3 dead Imperials to every 1 Fremen) when fighting in the desert. Learning the Fremen ways seems to be the most important adaptation in the novel; Paul couldn't have survived and taken back the Imperial city without it. Paul's adaptations through the novel were directly related to the plot.  It shows that people cannot survive in an environment without adequately changing to meet that environment's requirements.  Ultimately, the book shows how the better-adapted fremen with Paul beat the unready Imperial forces and Harkonnens.  This novel very effectively combines great descriptions of the setting and characters, with an intricate plot that utilizes the thoroughness of its characters and setting.  It is a masterpiece of science fiction and I recommend it to anyone who has to time to read a long novel.

B000000H6D|5.0|First and foremost, Type O Negative are NOT a goth band. People who perceive them as such are misled in their understanding of the genre and on Type O's music overall. Having said that, it is impossible to classify this band and this album in particular. Going from sabbath-esque dirges one moment to full blown prog rock the next, Peter Steele and his cohorts have trouble in picking a style and sticking to it. Perhaps this is why success has eluded the band for so long: every album is filled with contradictions and irony, eclecticism and variety. Unfortunately the record buying public is largely comprised of media worshippers and MTV dictated zombies. But that's getting off topic; back to the review.<br />Bloody Kisses is their greatest album. It combines heavy rock with pop, psychadelia, hardcore, ambient, stoner and classic rock, all enveloped in an atmosphere of darkness and tension (where the gothic connection arises from). Bloody Kisses is an LP which contains elements from several of the great rock giants of the past, twisting the sound and sending it into the post grunge age and injecting a modern thematic approach. The obvious influences are the Beatles, Black Sabbath, the Doors, Pink Floyd, etc. There are also new wave elements such as Duran Duran, Depeche Mode, the Cocteau Twins, My Bloody Valentine and hundreds other lesser known pop/rock bands of the 70's, 80's and 90's. The songs range in length from 12 minutes (Bloody Kisses) to 3 minutes (Kill all the White Pople). Perhaps the most under rated song from their catalog is &quot;Cant Lose You&quot;. It's the epic closer, a six minute ode to bereavement, an eerie, psychadelic and ambient drone awash with the mystical sounds of the east, with the sitar, tabla and tamboura being the prominent instruments therein. &quot;Christian Woman&quot; is possibly the greatest Type O song of all time, another epic containing three sections including a beautifully melodic acoustic folk section and a killer guitar riff toend it off. Everyone has heard &quot;Black Number One&quot;, the hard rock classic with an almost AC/DC-esque chorus and hilarious lyrics. &quot;Blood and Fire&quot; is another classic, with its good ol'Rock'n'Roll vibe and pump your fist in the air feeling. Great stuff.<br />Overall, Bloody Kisses sums up the best of Type O Negative. While their other albums do offer something worth listening to, none of them have the consistensy and confidence that oozes from this one. One of the greatest albums of the 90's, without a doubt. Open minds should embrace it, take it with a pinch of salt, and listen to some of the most creative and beautiful musical arrangements ever.<br />Take care and God bless.

The time meter is very screwed up so you can't really 'mosh' to it, which I find very ironic and fitting. :) Forces you to LISTEN to the music and words.<br />10) Disposition - Intro to quite possibly the most intense... movement (I won't call it a song, it's too incredible to be one).. that I've ever heard.<br />11) Reflection - Listen to this at 2AM, with the lights off, with headphones on. Is it me, or did Trent's touring with APC rub off on Tool here. GODLY stuff here, man! Best Tool I've ever heard.<br />12) Triad - very hard outro to end the trilogy. No lyrics - just lots of hard percussion and guitars/bass.<br />13) Faaip De Oiad - Gaping Lotus Project/Disgustipated reminscent. Fun for the whole family.<br />This was so easily worth the five year wait - I can't begin to express how I felt when I purchased this on the 14th (my local record store sells stuff early sometimes, hehe). If you haven't bought this album yet, there's something wrong with you. This will go down in history with Pink Floyd's &quot;Dark Side of the Moon&quot;. Get it!@!@!@

B00005B36H|5.0|I can say, without a doubt, that Lateralus is already my favorite Tool release.  Considering Tool is my favorite band and I love all of their previous releases, that is quite an endorsement -- but that's how incredible this album is.  It takes the best parts of Opiate, Undertow, and Ænima and seamlessly merges them for what can only be referred to as &quot;the ultimate rock album.&quot;  It has the savage, lacerating properties of Opiate in many places, the aggression of Undertow, and the depth of Ænima (without the muddy, more layered production of the latter).<br />Some reviewers have said that this album doesn't &quot;rock as hard&quot; as previous releases.  I don't know what they've been listening to, because two of the songs on here (&quot;The Grudge&quot; and &quot;Ticks and Leeches&quot;) are among the hardest rocking, if not the two hardest rocking, tracks Tool has ever laid down.  &quot;The Grudge&quot; will absolutely pulverize you the first, and each subsequent, time you hear it, and &quot;Ticks and Leeches&quot; will frighten you to the point where you curl up in a fetal position.  <br />This is not, however, some heavy metal screamfest (although Maynard's screams on &quot;Ticks and Leeches&quot; are about as violent, vitriolic, and nasty as they come); there are plenty of great SONGS on here.  &quot;Lateralus,&quot; for instance, is a great song; it boasts better musicianship than even Tool have served up before and great vocals (duh) that are intermittently melodic and aggressive.  &quot;The Patient&quot; almost sounds like a continuation of &quot;H.&quot; to me; it is truly wonderful.<br />In fact, everything on this CD is incredible, even the several interludes, like &quot;Eon Blue Apocalypse&quot; and &quot;Mantra.&quot;  The former is almost entirely acoustic, and provides an excellent segueway into &quot;The Patient.&quot;  Another great segueway is the change from &quot;Parabol&quot; to &quot;Parabola.&quot;  &quot;Parabol&quot; is a song, butit also acts as a sort of interlude; it is basically a shorter, softer version of &quot;Parabola,&quot; with some of the same lyrics.  The ending starts to get a little louder, and leads directly into the pounding opening of &quot;Parabola.&quot;  It is simply awesome.<br />I sometimes like to name &quot;standout tracks&quot; when reviewing a CD, but I can safely say that all of these tracks are great.  There is something for every Tool fan, as well as every fan of rock music in general.<br />Whatever your expectations before buying this album, rest assured, it will exceed them.  I've been waiting for this for more than four-and-a-half years (Ænima was released October 1, 1996), so you can imagine how great I imagined it would be when I finally got my hands on it; well, it completely blew me away.  You truly have to experience it to appreciate it.

B000002BMV|5.0|With the departure of founding member Greg Rolie and the advent of Jonathan Cain, Journey practically becomes a different band. Maybe it's that I was an early teenager when I discovered this record and grew up listening to it, but this album speaks to my subconscious. It could be my appreciation for Journey beyond their classic hits but my favorites on this album are 'Lay It Down', 'Mother, Father' and 'Escape'. (I'll correct this if I'm wrong, but I think Neal Schon's father co-wrote 'Mother, Father'.) You could see it in their live performances. They weren't quite sure what people would think. It's almost like when Steve Perry joined the band - but if you think Steve Perry made Journey sound mainstream ... The hereto underrealized pop songwriting ability of of Cain still seems a bit tentative here but the rich earthy sound of the Moog and the Hammond B3 are gone. The updated definitive 80s sound of the Sequential and Oberheim synthesizers are a world apart. Steve Perry is of course a tenor, but on this album his voice takes on a fuller, almost raspy character. He still sings in the same range as ever but he somehow makes it sound even easier than he did before. I still have my LP, which I pull out from time to time but I imagine I'll always have it on CD or on whatever other media this album becomes available in the future.

B000002OX3|3.0|Coming after the fierce &quot;maximum R&amp;B&quot; adrenaline of &quot;My Generation,&quot; &quot;A Quick One&quot; sounds a little pale. This is largely due to a business deal negotiated before the album's sessions had begun by the band's managers Chris Stamp and Kit Lambert. Even though Pete Townshend was the only member of the group that had done a serious amount of songwriting up to that point, Stamp and Lambert negotiated a cash advance against the publishing royalties for Keith Moon, John Entwistle and Roger Daltry. Each of the latter three members were to contribute two songs to the band's upcoming album. Apparently the cash advance was a pretty nice sum because Roger bought a Volvo with his share. <br />Sure, it sounded like a great idea at the time, but the actual execution didn't quite go as smoothly. Entwistle had a natural gift for writing and, unsurprisingly, turned in two better-than-average tunes, &quot;Boris the Spider&quot; and &quot;Whiskey Man.&quot; Keith Moon's two contributions are about as insane as his personality. &quot;I Need You&quot; is a nice enough song even though the drums are pushed WAY TOO HIGH up in the mix during the choruses. And &quot;Cobwebs and Strange&quot; sounds like a high school marching band on acid. However, it does feature a fierce drum break from Keith that is one of his most impressive showcases. Roger couldn't even muster up two songs and the one he came up with, &quot;See My Way,&quot; is very forgettable. Throw in a bland remake of &quot;Heatwave&quot; and four decent contributions from Pete, including his first ever stab at an extended, suite-like rock piece, &quot;A Quick One, While He's Away,&quot; and you've got yourself a very short, uneven album from The Who. <br />But the original album only takes up about half of the CD's running time. The other 10 tracks consist of the &quot;Ready Steady Who&quot; EP, some various B-sides and a few unreleased songs. These tracks range from novelty (&quot;Batman,&quot; &quot;Barbara Ann,&quot; &quot;Bucket T&quot;) to dark humor (&quot;Doctor, Doctor&quot;) to throwaways (&quot;In the City,&quot; &quot;I've Been Away) with a few classics (&quot;Disguises,&quot; &quot;Man With the Money.&quot;) <br />Far from an essential Who album, &quot;A Quick One&quot; does have some very good moments, but also contains some of the band's least insprired moments on record.

B000002KGT|5.0|Five fine musicians peaking musically, three young songwriters hitting their stride thematically, and two troubled couples bottoming out romantically, all at exactly the same moment in the same band, only months after making their entrance to the upper echelon of the charts with their previous LP.<br />How could RUMOURS miss?<br />There has never been a finer study of male-female interaction on vinyl, with the exception of Richard & Linda Thompson's SHOOT OUT THE LIGHTS, than RUMOURS.  Every base is covered, from both sides of the gender line, and no easy answers are given.  As in any devasting breakup--and, from all accounts, the splits of the McVies and Buckingham/Nicks were as intense as they come--fingers are pointed, nerves are frayed, accusations are made, and everyone gets the blame.  Apparantly a dreadful time for them all.<br />But for us, what a great, great record.  For all the destruction going on in the lives of individual members the band, as a band, just cooks. They surge and soar through \\"Don't Stop,\\" they throb and ache on \\"Dreams,\\" \\"The Chain,\\" and \\"Gold Dust Woman.\\"  <br />Nicks takes a timeout from the over-the-top pop-mysticism she displayed on FLEETWOOD MAC and submerged herself in after RUMOURS to deliver her best performance.  John McVie and Mick Fleetwood provide a rock solid bottom and Christine McVie, she of the endless stream of slow ballads, is nearly uptempo.<br />But the star of the hour, the true workhorse of RUMOURS, is Lindsey Buckingham.  From the opening lines of \\"Second Hand News\\" (\\"I know there's nothing to say, someone has taken my place...I'm never going back again\\") this LP swirls around his struggles at the end of his relationship with Nicks.  His guitar work, simply flash and finery prior to RUMOURS, is all about guts and lightning now.  <br />Despite all of the 'hope for the future' posturing propped up around Fleetwood Mac nostalgia these days, I've always considered \\"Go Your Own Way\\" to be their finest moment.  The firstfew moments of the tune are innocent enough--a steady strum from Buckingham's guitar, even a bit of acoustic harmony--but then something goes wrong:<br />\\"If loving you isn't the right thing to do...\\"  and McVie and Fleetwood enter, their drums and bass sounding like thunder on the horizon.  From there on out it is Buckingham's inner turmoil (\\"Tell me why everything turned around\\") and the band is just on fire in the chorus, full throated and full throttle.<br />There's a moment during the guitar solo when the band seems to be trying to hold itself together, to keep from flying off in so many directions, even Buckingham holds back to a few notes...and then the chorus, and this guitar break at the end that is so full of fire and grit and determination that it just brings tears to my eyes.   It's the hottest lead I've ever heard on a Pop hit, and it ends with a crash so powerful and so final that it changed my entire opinion of Lindsey Buckingham. <br />To me, \\"Go Your Own Way\\" is 3 minutes 31 seconds that sums up so much of the end of that mid-70s California narcissim, so much better than the Eagles ..., that it qualifies as one of the 20 greatest singles of all time.

B00005BL2A|5.0|After a decade of either retreading what they had already done (the often lovely Ultra) or what everyone else had already done (the rock-out-grunge-anthemic-opus Songs Of Faith And Devotion), Depeche Mode have finally worked through their &quot;problem years&quot; of David Gahan's drug habits and the departure of chief arranger/producer Alan Wilder.  They prove this to their legions of fans and the world with the brilliant, moving Exciter. <br />    Don't get me wrong... I love Songs Of Faith And Devotion (it was the album that got me hooked on DM) and Ultra.  I played them both to death and am attached to each and every note on those albums.  But I, and I have a feeling that the rest of DM's fans felt similarly, was worried that the DM gang had possibly dried up.  I couldn't have been more wrong. <br />    Exciter plays like the album it should - it's the kind of album DM fans should only dream of hearing.  Martin's songwriting, while familiar, still holds that element of surprise.  Take the tenth track, &quot;Breathe&quot; - a '50s sounding torch/blues song that finds Martin crooning softly about rumors, biblical images, and alibis.  Everytime we think we have DM pegged, Martin pulls something like this... it was country-tinged ballad &quot;The Bottom Line&quot; on Ultra and sweeping &quot;One Caress&quot; on SOFAD.  Meanwhile, most of the rest of the album plays with the texture of blues, goth, electronica, rock, and pop... something we expect from Martin. <br />   &quot;The Sweetest Condition&quot; uses a slide guitar over David and Martin's bluesy harmonies, and paints a gorgeously longing nighttime scene.  &quot;Goodnight Lovers&quot; is subtle, intense and moving, Dave's vocals adding just the right amount of nuance to the simple, downsloped melody line.  &quot;Shine&quot; is both beautiful and intense, and the chorus (&quot;You've been hanging from a rope of mediocrity/ Strung up by your insecurities...&quot;) is truly one of the highlights of this stunningalbum.  &quot;Freelove&quot; sounds like an instant DM classic, combining David's yearning vocals with a soft melody and arpeggiated guitar. <br />    Song for song, this is probably the most consistent album that DM has done since Violator and maybe even (my personal favorite) Black Celebration.  Each song sounds different from the one before it, and Exciter simply flows from one song to the next in the way that those two albums did.  The textures on Exciter (meaning the production, rhythm, and melody) remind me a lot of what Black Celebration would sound like if it were cross-bred with Violator.  I think a lot of the arrangements and keyboards sound like the harmonies used on Black Celebration (For instance, &quot;Comatose&quot; feels and sounds much the same way that &quot;World Full Of Nothing&quot; did - it breaks its verses and chorus into pieces with its bizarre harmonies; likewise, the easy-going riff on &quot;Freelove&quot; screams of &quot;Black Celebration&quot;'s swirling dissonances.).  But Violator is echoed in the content of Exciter's lyrics (love, devotion = sin, filth) and in the simplicity of the songs. <br />....  For my money, Exciter is a contender for &quot;the best DM album ever,&quot; along with Violator and Black Celebration.  And, in case you were wondering, its a great introduction to one of the most inventive, influential bands of the last 20 years. <br /> But give yourself time... Exciter grows on you.  Don't expect to love it the first time you hear it.  Exciter is a very subtle, slow-moving album - there's not a whole lot of shakin' goin' on here.  And the production may seem strange and out of place at first.  But once it works its magic on you (I'd give it 5 plays or so) be prepared to leave Exciter in your player for a while... Its simply fantastic.

B000000W5B|4.0|It was inevitable that, sooner or later, the Stones would record an album that didn't quite live up to expectations. By the time the world had gotten through digesting EXILE..., those expectations were extremely high. It didn't help that Keith, perhaps burnt out by long hours in the studio doing EXILE..., as well as his bad habits, seems to have largely phoned this one in. Sure enough, almost everybody jumped all over GOATS HEAD SOUP. Heard strictly on its own merits, though, I think GOATS... is a darn good piece of work.  With only a small contribution from Keith, the album leans heavily on Jagger for songs and Taylor for guitars. It also utilizes keyboards to a greater extent than most Stones albums. Nevertheless, the cuts here are very listenable.<br />&quot;Dancing With Mr. D&quot; is a solid rocker. Given its devilish theme, it suffers by comparison to &quot;Sympathy For The Devil&quot;. Rightly so.  But it's still not bad. &quot;Heartbreaker&quot; is a powerful song, although it's ironic to hear guys who employed Hells Angels for security at Altamont, getting a guy killed as a result, coming down on the NYPD. &quot;Coming Down Again&quot; and &quot;Winter&quot; are pretty ballads. The former, in particular, is Keith's moment. It also features some nice piano passages. &quot;Angie&quot; is a rather pretty song, as well, but that's one too many pretty songs for me, and Mick's vocal here sounds a bit gushy. Ballads aren't his forte, and he's better off giving them a no-frills treatment ( as with &quot;Ruby Tuesday&quot;, &quot;Backstreet Girl&quot; or &quot;Lady Jane&quot;). &quot;Silver Train&quot;, &quot;Hide Your Love&quot; and &quot;Can You Hear The Music&quot; are all good songs. I especially like the somewhat exotic sound of the latter. It's unlike anything else they've done and it grows on you (or on me, at least).<br />Then there is &quot;Star Star&quot;. This is at once the best and worst song of the bunch. It's a dynamite rocker (with a nod to Chuck Berry) driven by some wonderful rhythm guitar and a leering, sneering, all-out vocal by Mick. But the Stones had always walked a thin lyrical line in the past, using suggestive phrases and double entendre without descending to the openly vulgar. &quot;Star Star&quot; doesn't just cross that line, it obliterates it. Lyrically, the song is one long four-letter word. Further, it sets an unfortunately crude precedent for the future. And it's really not necessary. A little sublety would have worked just as well. It's not that I can't handle it. I'm a big boy. But my wife doesn't appreciate it, and I certainly don't want my ten-year-old daughter to hear it. So, I have to be careful where and when I play it, and headphones are usually called for. No airplay, either. A waste of a great song.<br />GOATS HEAD SOUP is an inevitable step down from its immediate predecessors, but it's still a very good album. There are plenty of bands around that would love to record something this good. Give it a try. I recommend it.ry) driven by some wonderful rhythm guitar and a leering, sneering, all-out vocal by Mick. But the Stones had always walked a thin lyrical line in the past, using suggestive phrases and double entendre without descending to the openly vulgar. &quot;Star Star&quot; doesn't just cross that line, it obliterates it. Lyrically, the song is one long four-letter word. Further, it sets an unfortunately crude precedent for the future. And it's really not necessary. A little sublety would have worked just as well. It's not that I can't handle it. I'm a big boy. But my wife doesn't appreciate it, and I certainly don't want my ten-year-old daughter to hear it. So, I have to be careful where and when I play it, and headphones are usually called for. No airplay, either. A waste of a great song.<br />GOATS HEAD SOUP is an inevitable step down from its immediate predecessors, but it's still a very good album. There are plenty of bands around that would love to record something this good. Give it a try. I recommend it.

B00005BL2A|2.0|First let me clarify that I LOVE DM and have since 84.  Perhaps that is the root of the problem I have with their latest album.  The things that make DM great are missing.  Dave's once powerful and unique voice has been manipulated by a vocal coach and clearly represents some level of conformity of what constitutes a &quot;good voice.&quot;  The once insightful, socially aware and cynical lyrics have completely vanished.  The sappy love songs and cheesy addiction themes on this album are played out and represent an either uninspired Martin or a group of once prolific old men whose children have sucked their creativity.  Some of these songs are simply ridiculous. The time signatures and meter of these songs are tired and unimaginative.  And finally, the once cohesive Depeche Mode album has given way to a collection of someone else's vision.  It is clear and painfully obvious that when Depeche Mode produces the album, or at least part of it, its good.  When someone else does, as is the case with this one, it lacks DM's thumbprint - and without that, there's no point in calling it Depeche Mode.
B00005AR3G|5.0|THE EMPEROR'S NEW GROOVE is the funniest movie that I have seen in years. I initially had passed on watching it in the theater because I thought it looked childish, but was convinced to give it a try now that it was out on video. Boy, was my initial impression wrong.<br />The story revolves around a selfish prince who is turned into a llama by one of his subjects. With the help of a kindly peasant he attempts to regain his throne, and in the process finds there's more to him than he previously thought or wanted there to be. While the moral is a good one, it really plays a secondary role to the comedy, which is literally non-stop. GROOVE takes the rapid-fire humor that Disney perfected in Aladdin and combines it with cartoon-style hijinx, the wit of David Spade, and a hilarious lack of reverence for the traditional Disney animated genre. Spade plays his role to perfection, and luckily never crosses the line into being obnoxious. Seinfeld fans are in for a treat, as Puddy plays a very special side-kick role. You really can't take your eyes off the screen for a minute, because you're guaranteed to miss a gag of some sort. The humor works mainly on an adult level, even though kids will be rolling from the funny visuals and some of the more obvious jokes.<br />The DVD itself is a good one. The digital transfer was excellent and the sound quality is top notch. The commentary track is one of the better that I've heard, with interesting and funny insight from all contributors. Animated menus. There's a deleted scene that was probably best left out, but is interesting to watch anyway, plus a few other fun features.<br />In the end, this is one of the best Disney flicks to come along in years. Hardcore Disney fans will eat it up. But even if you're not a fan of Disney's usual animated features you should give GROOVE a shot. Cast aside your pre-concieved notions and you just might be in for a very pleasant surprise. I was. FIVE STARS.

B00003W8NM|5.0|It is far too easy for the general populace to misunderstand this movie, I guess.  So many people seem to be missing the point - indeed, *any* point - that this movie could be making, instead complaining about the violence and nihilism.  Could it be that the message actually *did* hit home, but most people were so let down - or ticked off - by it, that they decided it wasn't there?<br />This movie is the flipside of &quot;American Beauty,&quot; another great film in which anarchy is used as an escape from the drudgery of life.  &quot;Beauty,&quot; though, was praised and celebrated, while &quot;Fight Club&quot; shunned.  Why?  The answer may be quite simple:  &quot;Beauty&quot; actually encouraged such behavior, appealing to the modern mantra &quot;if it feels good, do it.&quot;  Consequences were ignored, leaving one feeling that this was the right thing to do.  &quot;Fight Club,&quot; on the other hand, took that mantra to it's natural, disturbing end.  As Edward Norton yells late in the movie &quot;You morons!  You're running around in ski masks, blowing stuff up!  What did you expect?&quot;  While &quot;Beauty&quot; was a call to freedom, &quot;Fight Club&quot; was a call to moderation.  People are afraid to deal with consequences, though, therefore they focus on everything else.<br />As for the violence, this is no worse than any boxing movie I've ever seen, with the exception of two gun-related wounds and one severe beating.  And from &quot;Raging Bull&quot; to &quot;Rocky,&quot; not a complaint has arisen.  Why now?  Because there's a message involved.  What I don't understand is how a film like Kubrick's &quot;A Clockwork Orange,&quot; is considered a challenging, thought-provoking classic, when it's even *more* disturbing and over the top.  At least &quot;Fight Club&quot; doesn't feature any giant genetalia.<br />Everything involved in this movie is top notch, from Edward Norton's bored, cynical narration to David Fincher's visually stunning direction.  Indeed, this film has such a bizzarre energy, warping in and out of speed and giving new meaning to the phrase &quot;extreme close-up.&quot;  There's enough here to please most people looking for something new in film.<br />The most wonderful thing about this movie is the witty, literate screenplay by Jim Uhls, based on the equally stunning novel by the Vonnegut-like Chuck Pahlnuiuhk.  Mixing humor, action, and subtle (and not-so-subtle) philosophy, this just might be the most quotable movie of the year.<br />Now, for the DVD.  When a movie is the reason you buy a DVD player, that says something.  When said movie is actually *worth* the DVD player, that says even more.  Wonderful for fans, this movie gives some wonderful behind the scenes material and stories.  Especially fun is the alternate audio track with Fincher, Norton, Brad Pitt, and Helena Bonham Carter (though her track was recorded seperately).  It's obvious the bond Fincher, Norton, and Pitt made during the shooting, given the relaxed atmosphere of this audio.  More than once a story is told about a botched take, and like kids in a candy shop, Norton and Pitt beg Fincher to put it on the DVD.  Jokes fly, complaints about the negative reviews, thoughts on the movies themes, and this is never dull to listen to.  This is far more then can be said about some other movies (&quot;Hollow Man,&quot; for instance; could anyone take such a fluff movie more seriously?)  Personally, I can't wait to get back and listen to screenwriter Uhls and author Pahluniuhk duke it out!<br />Much like &quot;Clockwork Orange,&quot; this movie disturbed me, entertained me, made me laugh and think.  Unlike that classic, though, this is one I feel I can show my little brother, or my parents, and watch numerous times myself.<br />Check it out.

As a bonus, I liked this story.<br />The simple premise revolves around a nine year old lost in a huge woods with only her wits and a few supplies to sustain her.  Her wits devolve upon Red Sox pitcher Tom Gordon, her favorite player, whose image accompanies her.  The imagined Gordon provides the inner voice she needs to meet the challenges of the deep woods and her own mind.  Of course, there also lurks the unknown terror at the edge of the wood, but from King's reputation I assume that this nemesis provides much less horror than is usually found in his more famous works.  Rather, the unknown terror provides suspense and a motivating force that this child must deal with.  More would give away the crux of the story.  Suffice to say the reader won't be disappointed by the end of the book.<br />Simple, but King's excellent writing sustains this simple premise over two hundred and fifty odd pages.  His character development is wonderful -- one feels the little girl's (Trisha)emotions as she confronts her trials.  His dialogue (in flashbacks) feels real and the whole story is believable.<br />This book works and is an excellent quick read.

It incorporates classical elements, jazz, pop, rock, etc.  The most impressive thing to me, however, are the lyrics.  I've always been impressed by DM's lyrics, but for a different reason.  Before, I was impressed by their apparently exhaustive collection of rhyming dictionaries.  Very clever rhymes, very tightly constructed.  I say this as a published poet myself. 0517548232|5.0|I teach Principles of Microeconomics, and I always use this book for extra credit. Students who hate reading long, boring, stuffy text books always like Hazlitt, and give him high reviews every single semester. The very readable chapters are short (about 3-6 pages in most cases), and told in story form to make Hazlitt's point. This makes it possible for even freshmen with notoriously short attention spans to read the day's chapter.<br />Hazlitt's &quot;one lesson&quot; is simple, and told in Chapter 1. The rest of the chapters are all stories in which the lesson plays a prominent role. In short, Hazlitt doesn't merely tell us the lesson, he actually shows us the lesson -- over and over and over, until we've got it.<br />With stories on tariffs, minimum wage, rent controls, taxes. unions, wages, profits, savings, credit, unemployment, and so much more, Hazlitt takes some of the most difficult economic concepts and makes these easily accessible to the lay person who has no economic training, background, or even inclination.<br />It's one  thing for me to recommend this book. It's quite another for my students to recommend it semester after semester. I can imagine no higher praise.

B00000373U|5.0|Like a lot of people I first got into Elliot Smith's music after \\" Good Will Hunting\\" and his Oscars appearance. A lot of press stuff at that time though was referring back to the more basic, less produced, sound of \\"Either/Or\\" as Elliot's best work, so I got that one too.  Now this record really is an acquired taste, you have to work at it. At first it seemed so slight as to be almost not there, similar in style to an unplugged \\"Murmur\\" period REM with Michael Stipe in major mumble mode, or the odd Big Star quiet number.  I played it to a friend who was a particular fan of early REM and he dismissed \\"Either/Or\\" as sounding like \\"A nerdy Simon and Garfunkel\\", I'd never noticed that similarity at all.  After a few listens, the songs on \\"Either/Or\\" don't just get under your skin, they weedle their way into your very soul, you start to think things like \\"this is mine, my life is in here\\".  What it is that is so affecting about this record is it's apparent innocence, Elliot picks away very ably at his guitar, the light breathy vocals drift in and out, but the tone of the songs is deceptive.  Such a style suggests quiet introspection, and singer songwriterly naval gazing, but with repeated listening, a full range of emotions, retelling of events and even stories seep through. Oh and this man gets angry and he curses, and let me tell you, nobody curses with such venom and purpose as Elliot.  It's not a particularly long record, but you do find if you put it on at the beginning you stay till the end, it's not a record from which you just pick a stand out or two.  So I don't have favourite songs on this one, just moments, like the bass riff that pulls you in mid way through \\"No Name No.5\\", and the line \\"You'll be the victim of your own dirty tricks\\" from \\"Pictures of Me\\" and the annoyance in Elliot's voice when he's telling himself off in \\"2.45 am\\". It's a wonderful and rewarding record.

B000002P2W|5.0|Although The Who bravely, and perhaps unwisely, soldiered on after the untimely and senseless death of drum genius Keith Moon in 1978, it was painfully obvious by the release of &quot;The Who By Numbers&quot; in 1975 that the band's glory years were sadly drawing to a conclusion. On the plus side, Moon, never the most conventional of timekeepers, shows why he was the greatest drummer in rock and roll history, filling every empty moment with thunderous rolls and fills, almost as if attempting to prop up the sinking Townshend. B00003CXR1|4.0|FEAST AND FABLE<br />Movie Review of Chocolat<br />Chocolat is a sumptuous feast for those with a cinematic sweet tooth. A single mother (Juliette Binoche) driven by the restless wanderlust of her Navaho mother's spirit, moves to a quiet French town with her six year old daughter and opens a chocolate shop in the middle of Lent, the Catholic fast before Easter. Her free spirited atheism enrages the sanctimonious mayor (Alfred Molina), and a battle commences for the hearts and minds of the villagers.<br />There is magic swirling around Binoche and her chocolatery, blurring the lines between real and unreal and giving the story the feel of a fable.  However the gritty performance of Lena Olin as a battered wife keeps it from floating too far from earth. Swedish director Lasse Hallström (My Life as a Dog) once again paints a convincingly chilly portrait of small town life, aided by the capable acting of Binoche, Judi Dench and Johnny Depp in particular.  Be warned, however, the real star of this movie is the chocolate. Don't go on an empty stomach or you may find yourself worryingly distracted from the narrative flow. The story is light on dramatic urgency, but the cast stirs in their own spicy fruit into the half-baked script and the result is delicious enough to satisfy the most gluttonous of appetites.

B00003CXB2|5.0|<br />Since previous reviewers have covered the DVD-specific aspects of this item pretty thoroughly I hope you'll understand why I'm reviewing the film contents rather than the physical presentation.<br /><br />T.E. Lawrence was, in several senses, a bastard.<BR> He was the illegitimate son of a well-to-do Englishman who contributed financially to Lawrence's upbringing - but never had the faintest intention of formally recognising their relationship.<BR> This, at time when class and parentage were key pillars of a totally hypocritical social system, goes a long way to explaining Lawrence's ambiguous feelings about his own identity (he used several different names at one time or another), even down to his *allegedly* waivering sexuality.<br /><br />It may also help to explain why Lawrence was so drawn to the vast emptiness of the Arabian desert, despite his upbringing in England's &quot;green and pleasant land&quot;.<br /><br />In England, Lawrence had little option but to be whatever society made of him.  In Arabia he was able to be whatever he cared to make of himself.  (Which was no doubt why he had already spent quite some time in the Middle East before WW1 started - mainly on archaeological activities.)<BR> Thus the word play between Sherif Ali (Omar Sharif) &quot;It is written...&quot; and Lawrence (O'Toole) &quot;Nothing is written unless I write it&quot; has a significance far beyond Lawrence's sheer determination to return into the searing heat on 'The Anvil' to rescue Gasim (though there was indeed a real-life incident on which the episode was based).<br /><br />The subsequent episode in which Lawrence executes Gasim is not only about the metaphysical consideration of whether you can buck your fate.  It also refers us back to this question of just how far a man can truly control his own destiny in the face of social pressures.<BR> I personally believe that this brilliantly understated 'sub-text' is one of the primary reasons why the film has remained so successful for so long.<br /><br />A second, though less subtle, sub plot is linked to the nature of British Imperialism.<br /> When this film was made, most people living in Britain still remembered at least a part of the age of the great British Empire.  At the same time, however, with lands and colonies being stripped away at an ever-increasing rate, we were already entering the unknown `territory' of post-Colonialism.<br /><br />In part this meant a lingering pride in our previous power - when &quot;half the world was coloured red&quot; - but also a growing belief, in some quarters, that our main contribution to the countries we'd ruled had been to rip them off for as much of their wealth as we could lay our little hands on.<br /><br />In Victorian times we had justified our behaviour by elevating Darwinism - and particularly &quot;the Survival of the Fittest&quot; - to the level of both scientific and moral absolutes, thus excusing virtually any amount of disrespect to the natives of those countries we ruled over.<br /><br />With this idea in mind it is interesting to watch the interplay between the quintessential 'white man', Lawrence (even down to the colour of his robes) and the Arab 'hordes'.  Note, for example, Lawrence's several announcements that the Arabs shall have this or that because *he* will give it to them.  In reality, of course, it was Lawrence who was utterly dependent for his success.<BR> This contrast of Arab and Westerner hits home hardest, I think, in the scenes in Damascus, both in the Arab Parliament and in the Turkish hospital.  But it is not entirely one-sided.<br /><br />In the final scene in Allenby's office we find the General, the Machiavellian civil servant `Mr' Dryden (brilliantly portrayed by Claude Raines) and King Feisal (Alec Guinness) in conference.  It is they who will discuss and determine the future of Arabia, whilst Lawrence is `bumped up' to Colonel - and shipped home to England (where he can no longer cause any problems).<br /><br />By the way, although, in the film, Lawrence's funeral follows straight on from his return from Egypt, there was in reality a gap of approx. 18 years between the two events, during which he tried hard to escape from his fame by joining the RAF (1922) as J H Ross, a mechanic, then the Tank Corps (1923) as T E Shaw, and back to the RAF in 1925.  By the time of his fatal accident he had retired to a country cottage in Dorset.<br /><br />As a Brit. I find the film endlessly enthralling, and David Lean's direction is as profound and intelligent as you will find in ANY film from any country at any time.<br /><br />I wonder how it looks to non-Brits  8¬)

Rush balanced their individual musicianship in these songs, and the style of songs balanced their progressive roots with a more efficient 80's approach.  Importantly, the keyboards still balanced with Alex's guitars, and did not overwhelm the soundspace.  Oh yes, Neil's drums were very well recorded here...very clear and resonant!<br />I actually hated &quot;Tom Sawyer&quot; the first time I put the needle to the vinyl, and I've never really liked it much since...(with the exception of &quot;The Spirit of Radio&quot;, I don't tend to like Rush's lead-off radio promo songs).  <br />&quot;Red Barchetta&quot; is kind of a musical storytelling experience...nicely done!  Who cares if it's only about a car?  Rush is allowed to write about mundane things once in a while.<br />&quot;YYZ&quot; is a flashy instrumental with, admittedly, a disco-esque feel.  Very jammin' though, and the incorporation of the morse code for the Tornto airport is quite cool!<br />&quot;Limelight&quot; is a very well-written song with a rich sound.  Gotta dig Neil banging that gong bass drum at the end!  Lyrically, it's a unique song from a Rock band.  How many Rock stars fear having &quot;insufficient tact&quot;?<br />&quot;The Camera Eye&quot;:  An earlier review said that this song could have been made at 3 1/2 minutes rather than 11.  I think it could have gone on for ANOTHER five minutes!  This is one of my favorite Rush songs.  11 minutes - hell yeah!!  The music has the time to map out a wonderful texture with full-bodied (gotta be a Gibson, heh) guitars blaring over nifty-sounding synth lines.  The double- and triple-tracked acoustic and electric guitars sound magnificent.  The drums groove through the whole thing with power and finesse.  Geddy's voice is just right.  The lyrics are vivid.  Ahh yes, play it again, I have another 11 minutes to spare!<br />&quot;Witch Hunt&quot; is like black tar spewing from your speakers.  A dark subject is perfectly illustrated by a heavy, brooding sound.  The multi-tracked drums are intense.<br />&quot;Vital Signs&quot; is OK, and offers a whole different feel to round out the album.  It's light, upbeat sound is in stark contrast to that of Witch Hunt.  I still think the Police did a much better job with reggae-rock than Rush ever did.  Still, not to be dismissed entirely.

0446347949|5.0|Although it has been many years since this book was first published (1993), it remains relevant to the contemporary business world and is probably more valuable now than ever before. It is truly a &quot;classic&quot; in company with Levitt's The Marketing Imagination and few others. The basic concept is that whatever you and your associates may think of what you sell, how it is perceived by those who are asked to buy is really what determines whether or not it will be purchased. Literally, the marketplace's perceptions (including those of competitors) are the decisive realities. To position a given product or service effectively, therefore, is to have it perceived to be superior to (or at least more desirable than) other purchase options. In an age when almost every product and service has become a commodity, &quot;customer service&quot; and &quot;brand equity&quot; have necessarily been re-defined but the principles affirmed by Ries and Trout remain unchanged. The challenge to marketers now is to differentiate with a more complicated multiple of components which include perceived value (rather than cost), perceived quality, perceived reliability (hence product or service credibility as well as credibility of seller), and perceived ease and convenience of doing business with the seller. There is one other component which should also be included: the pleasure of the purchase experience which Schmitt's explains brilliantly in Experiential Marketing as do Pine and Gilmore in The Experience Economy and Wolf in The Entertainment Economy.<br />When Ries and Trout wrote this book almost a decade ago, the positioning strategies they recommended were in response to a competitive marketplace quite different from the one they and we now survey. Nonetheless, the urgency of effective positioning remains...indeed is even greater today. This is one of the very few books on marketing which should be re-read at least every 3-6 months. (Ditto Levitt's book.) One final point: There is a direct correlation between customer expectations and their perceptions. As the former become higher (sometimes unrealistic), the latter change accordingly. Whatever you offer for sale, make certain that you fully understand customer expectations first before deciding how to manage customer perceptions. Those perceptions are within a frame-of-reference which constantly changes. Positioning is not a location; rather, a strategic imperative.s a direct correlation between customer expectations and their perceptions. As the former become higher (sometimes unrealistic), the latter change accordingly. Whatever you offer for sale, make certain that you fully understand customer expectations first before deciding how to manage customer perceptions. Those perceptions are within a frame-of-reference which constantly changes. Positioning is not a location; rather, a strategic imperative.

0783226063|3.0|It's best to think of this as more as an audio-visual feast than a proper film. 'Dune', the novel, is big and complex, requiring that the reader has an understanding of the far-future universe before he or she can grasp the complexities of the story (which, at heart, is an old-fashioned tale of feudal intrigue). Lynch adapts the book completely the wrong way - he stuffs in as many concepts and characters as he can, whilst removing large parts of the story. The upshot is that a lot of actors appear at the beginning of the film, introduce themselves, and then vanish, leaving us, the audience, wondering whether we were supposed to remember them, and wondering if they were important. Patrick Stewart, for example, appears for less than ten minutes, despite being quite a major character. But as something to look at and listen to it's superb. The production design is very reminiscent of the 1980 'Flash Gordon', but darker and more baroque, and is based in part on sketches by H R Giger (created for an aborted mid-70s version of the film), and the 'look' is almost uniformly perfect. The music, by arch-AOR session men Toto, is actually quite good, and there's a little bit of Brian Eno in there too. Patrick Stewart, Jurgen Prochnow and Francesca Annis (who looks very nice in a stillsuit) are pretty much definitive in their roles, whilst the rest of the actors range from good (McLachlan) to sort-of-okayish (Sting isn't all *that* bad, not that he has very much to do) to poor (Freddy Jones and that horrible little girl). Lynch himself was very disappointment with the result, and this might be the reason why the DVD itself doesn't have a great deal of extras (a trailer and some notes, essentially).

B00005B36H|5.0|I read somewhere a comment made by a person who preferred APC to Tool : &quot;They don't sound as Satanic.&quot; A ridiculous statement to be sure, but I couldn't help but reflect on that simpleminded thought listening to Aenima again, with its primal-scream, almost hateful lyrics, muddled production values, and evil-sounding interludes (&quot;Message to Harry Manback&quot;, &quot;+/- Ions&quot;, &quot;Die eier von satan&quot;). <br />But you don't get that sense with this album, the same drama is there, the same tension, even some of the same raw anger, but its much more coherent as a whole. And if, clocking in at a massive 78 minutes and 58 seconds, it rambles a bit at points, well, all the better to linger on a beautiful thought, don't you agree ? Listen to the lyrics Maynard sings over the deep and funky groove of Parabol/Parabola : &quot;Recognize this as a holy gift and celebrate this chance to be alive and breathing/Remember/We are eternal/All this pain is an illusion.&quot; Does this sound like the same Maynard who wrote Anenima, a corrosive fantasy about California dropping into the sea along with &quot;millions of dumbfounded dips****&quot; ? <br />And I don't know who slipped Danny Carey, resident mystic and &quot;batterie&quot; for Tool, the happy pill, but he is all over the place on this album. Bashing his skins like he has something to prove, and his life may just depend on it. Its the biggest drum sound I have ever heard on a rock album since Peter Gabriel and Jerry Marotta were doing the two-step in the early eighties.<br />&quot;The Grudge&quot;, the first King Crimson-like incantation on the album, is his most propulsive moment. At one point in this song, easily the most rhythmically complex thing Tool has ever attempted, he does three double-time tempo changes, one after the other, with double bass while pounding the snare.<br />My favorite moment on this album chock gosh-darn full of favorite moments is &quot;The Patient.&quot; In this song Maynard goes through all the emotions and moods and nails some absolutely riveting, majestic vocal harmonies. It represents the synthesis of all of the melodic possibilities that A Perfect Circle provided whilst sacrificing none of the near-thuggish, primal lurch that is the backbone of Tool. If the rest of the album had followed that direction, I would be more than satisfied with the 5-year wait, but instead Tool chooses to challenge the listener and take even more daring chances. There is the effectively venemous &quot;Ticks and Leeches&quot;, quite possibly the only song on this album you might be able to label a &quot;throwback&quot;; the catchy single &quot;Schism&quot; (which is either a paean to troubled lovers, or perhaps a statement of healing from Maynard to the rest of the members of Tool, after the success of his side project APC) punctuated by Justin Chancellor's fantastic complex bass riff; the lyrical juxtaposition of Parabol/Parabola; or the near 22-minute slow simmer, Eastern-tinged suite of Disposition, Reflection and Triad, which sports some stunning textural Adam Jones/Justin Chancellor interlocking guitar/bass interplay.<br />There comes a point at which all successful musicians eventually reach no matter what their fan base is, where they have to make music they write or perform that is more for their own gratification than others, or else they have to rehash old formulas and eventually become some worn-out retread on the oldies circle (see also : John Kay and Steppenwolf). If they take that step, they can only hope that a few of their fans do get it. Looking at nearly 80 minutes of music, I am fairly certain that many of the music press will throw out accusations of self-gratification - or even worse, the &quot;progressive&quot; label of doom. But this is not that album. Its not even close to that album. Tool is trying to say something; they have a rare but undeniable death-grip on something deep, powerful and primal and the even rarer ability tobring it through in their music. Its mathematical and complex, to be sure, but not erudite and inaccessible. It is exhilarating and mystical.

B000002KCC|5.0|In my senior year in high school (1975) I was a huge Neil Young fan.  I had all his solo albums, had seen him in concert several times, knew virtually every one of his tunes by heart, and I even looked like the guy.  When I heard that he had released a new album, &quot;Tonight's the Night&quot;, I hustled down to the local Tower Records, forked over my ...,and rushed home to give it listen.  I hated it.  &quot;What,&quot; I asked no one in particular, &quot;is this mess?&quot;  Neil's lack of polish and affectation were always part of his appeal, but &quot;Tonight's the Night&quot; was too much.  Neil and his band were so loaded they could hardly play, Neil singing was so bad it was laughable, and the whole record sounded like it had been recorded in a barn.  I dismissed the  thing as some kind of joke and filed &quot;Tonight's the Night&quot; away on the shelf.  I then put on &quot;After the Gold Rush&quot; and thought about what I could have bought with that ... I wasted on &quot;Tonight's the Night&quot;.<br />A few weeks later I was telling a friend how bad &quot;Tonight's the Night&quot; was and pulled it off the shelf to play for him.  But, on second listen the album didn't sound as bad as I had remembered.  &quot;Come Baby, Let's Go Downtown&quot; was a nice rockin' tune, &quot;Roll Another Number&quot; was an amusing druggie tale, and &quot;Tired Eyes&quot; seemed  like an sad, even pretty, ballad.  &quot;Hmmm,&quot; I thought &quot;Maybe this isn't a joke after all.&quot;  I still didn't think it was a good album, it sure wasn't &quot;Harvest&quot;, but I decided to give it a few more listens before I wrote it off for good.<br />So over the next few weeks I listened to &quot;Tonight's the Night&quot; again.  And again.  And again.  And again.  I listened to it drunk and I listened to it sober.  I listened to during the day and I listened to it at night.  I listened to it loud and I listened to it soft.  I just couldn't stop listening to it.  It replaced &quot;Live at Fillmore East&quot; as the album that was constantly on my turntable.  It was absolutely hypnotic.  &quot;Tonight's the Night&quot; sure wasn't &quot;Harvest&quot;; it was far better. It was some strange kind of Canadian, whiteboy, folk blues that reached right inside you, in the same way that Robert Johnson did, or Roscoe Holcomb did.  It was the real thing, and it didn't matter if Neil couldn't carry a tune to save his life, or that the steel guitar was out of tune; it was pure, unaffected emotion that could send chills down your spine.  And the songs!  Each one was gem and I soon knew them all by heart (and I still do).  Needless to say, my initial impression of &quot;Tonight's the Night&quot; underwent a complete revision.  I now thought it was a bloody masterpiece and I've had no reason to change my mind since then. B00005B36H|5.0|I should have written a review as soon as I received Lateralus, but I was just too busy listening!!! In addition, Lateralus shows me just how much talent resides in the entire band as composers as well as musicians.  The entire flow of the album from beginning to end is simply breathtaking.  I have not felt so captivated to listen to an album from beginning to end since Pink Floyd's Dark Side of the Moon.  In fact, this album truly reminds me of that other masterwork.  <br />Beginning with &quot;The Grudge,&quot; the album starts off like a rollercoaster, then flows through the beautiful and affecting bumps that the songs in the middle weave you in and out of, and concludes with a suite of tunes that conclude with &quot;Triad,&quot; one of the most battering and rhythmically fierce instrumentals ever.  I have never heard Carey drum like this.  His intricacies as a drummer really shine in Neal Peart-like precision.  Justin has surely beefed up his bass lines, and Jones' guitar textures are ever inventive, and as with Aenima, the genius of his riffs slowly begin to rise up through repeated listens.   Keenan, on the other hand, truly takes the inspired route of subtlety.  It seems almost as though Keenan uses his voice as an instrument more on this record.  His voice blends in both harmonically and rhythmically better with the band than in any other Tool record.  His lyrics are more emotional and deep than ever, and he truly shows melodic his melodic growth on &quot;Parabola&quot; and on the haunting &quot;Disposition.&quot;  <br />Perhaps my favorite track is the pseudo title track &quot;Lateralis.&quot;  This song is quite possibly the summation of everything that Tool has accomplished to this date:  Perfect lyrics, instrumental talent, unusual time meters, huge pulsating rhythmic sections, and a breathtaking emotional climax.  <br />Tool has delivered with their new album.  If you pick up one album all year this should be it.  One reviewer mentioned that this was Tool's most accessible album.  I disagree.  I find this their most challenging album initially, with it's huge song structures, yet the most rewarding in the end because the music only begs you to dig deeper, find the meaning for yourself, and let it seep into your mind and become a part of you.  Buy this album and you will not regret it.  However, I urge you to not simply HEAR this album.  You must LISTEN to it.  Listen to it 3 times in a row, uniterrupted, and you will see it's beauty.  Guaranteed.

0062502182|2.0|The Alchemist is a story in the tradition of the Arabian nights, woven from the same fabric of desert storms, brandished swords, and buried treasures. The protagonist, an Andalusian shepherd boy, sells his flock and travels to Africa to realize his dream of finding a great treasure. Along the way, he meets an old king named Melchitzedek, a wizened gypsy woman, a crystal merchant, and a sage alchemist, who teach him lessons about dreams and destiny drawn from the Hebrew Bible, Christian legend, and Muslim lore. If readers feel that they sensed the ending intuitively before turning the final page, then Coelho has achieved his aim, for the ultimate lesson of this book is about the intimate connections between what seems most distant and what lies deepest in our hearts. Even so, the aphoristic advice becomes tedious after a while and the injunction to \\"follow your dream\\" remains rooted in a mythical world where the sun speaks, the desert answers, and lead is converted, by magic, to gold.

0812589041|4.0|The novel Ender's Game is a great piece of work.  The story begins with young boy named Andrew &quot;Ender&quot; Wigin being chosen by his country to go off to battle school where he will learn to become a military genius.  While he is off at military school,his brother and sister are back at home building an alliance with each other. Ender goes through many different things throughout his times at the battle school.  Ender goes through peer pressure, the fact of not being treated as an equal,and isolation from the world.  The games are very wierd but in a great way.  The games are based on real life things that could happen to Ender but in a different point of view.    The practice battles are also very neat and some of the things Ender does during these practices are out of this world.  This book is a must read for all science fiction fans.
